The Distance Argument and Its Critique
Sanusi presents the sixth proof and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof against infinite regress and the flaw identified by Sanusi.

Core Idea
A finite distance between cause and effect does not prove a finite chain if intuition fails.

Sanusi now presents a sixth proof that attempts to show the finitude of a causal chain by considering the distance between an effect and its remote causes. He argues that this distance is bounded, so the whole chain cannot be infinite. But he immediately notes a flaw: the argument depends on an intuitive leap that may not apply to an infinite series. Let's examine both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Notice the concise style of Sanusi: he states the proof then adds a critical remark.
The proof uses the analogy of a journey measured in parasangs (فرسخ).
2
Understand the analogy
The proof says: if every segment of the distance between the effect and each remote cause does not exceed a parasang, then the total distance does not exceed a parasang plus a small part. Similarly, the causal chain cannot exceed a finite number plus one.
This relies on an intuition (حدس) that summing bounded segments yields a bounded total.
3
Identify the critique
Sanusi ends with 'وفيه نظر' (and there is consideration/flaw in it). This signals that the argument is not sound. The flaw is that the intuition may not hold for an infinite regress: the segments might be unbounded or the summation infinite.
The critique is brief but important – the author does not accept the proof as valid.
4
Reflect on the limitation
Ask yourself: Could an infinite series have bounded segments yet still be infinite? The answer is no if the bounding is uniform, but the intuition (hads) that 'the whole is finite plus one part' assumes the whole is already finite. The argument may be circular.
This lesson shows that even clever arguments against infinite regress require careful scrutiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس من إبطال التسلسل: ما بین هذا المعلول وكل من علله البعيدة متناه لكونه بين حاصرين فتتناهى السلسلة لأنها حينئذ لا تزيد على المتناهي إلا بواحد ضرورة أنه إذا لم يزد ما بين هذه المسافة وكل جزء منه على فرسخ لم يزد الكل على فرسخ إلا بجزء بحكم الحدس وفيه نظر.
The sixth proof for refuting infinite regress: What is between this effect and each of its remote causes is finite because it is between two limits, so the chain is finite because it then does not exceed the finite except by one, necessarily, because if what is between this distance and every part of it does not exceed a parasang, then the whole does not exceed a parasang except by a part, by intuition (hads). And there is consideration (flaw) in it.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth proof against infinite regress and the author's critical remark. The proof argues that the distance (in terms of causal ranks) between the last effect and any of its remote causes is finite because it is bounded between two endpoints. Therefore the entire series is finite, since it cannot exceed that finite distance by more than one. The proof uses an analogy: if each segment of a journey does not exceed one parasang, then the total journey does not exceed one parasang plus one small part. This is by intuition (hads). The author then states 'and there is consideration in it' (وفيه نظر), indicating a flaw: the argument assumes that the distances are uniformly bounded, but in an infinite regress, the distances might not be bounded in that way, or the intuition (hads) may not apply to an infinite series.

Key Terms
حاصرين
فرسخ
حدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The distance argument conclusively proves the finitude of the causal chain.
Correction: The author notes there is a flaw (نظر) because the argument relies on an intuition (حدس) that may not apply to infinite regress, and the reasoning may be circular.

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, what makes the chain finite?
A) The chain is even.
B) The distance between each cause and the effect is finite.
C) The chain cannot exceed a finite distance by more than one.
D) The chain is odd.

Key Takeaway
The distance argument attempts to prove the finitude of causal chains but is criticized for relying on an intuition that may not apply and for potential circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How often do you accept a belief about Allah because it 'feels right' or seems intuitive, without checking it against revelation? The distance argument seemed intuitive but was flawed. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah's actions in your life that you assume are limited or finite based on your own intuition? What would it take for you to surrender that assumption and trust Allah's infinite wisdom?
?
When Sanusi says 'wa fihi nazar', he shows that even a seemingly solid proof can have a hidden flaw. In your relationship with Allah, are you willing to critically examine your own reliance on logical reasoning when it comes to matters of faith? Do you trust Allah's unlimited power even when your mind says it seems impossible? What is one area where you need to let go of your intellectual comfort?
?
Reflect on a time when you felt that your distance from Allah was too great, or that there was a limit to how much He could forgive you. How does this lesson about finite distances and infinite series challenge that assumption? Are you putting a finite limit on Allah's infinite mercy based on your own flawed intuition? What step will you take today to break that assumption?

The Sixth Argument Against Infinite Regress: The 'Ma Bayn' Argument and Its Critique
Al-Taftazani presents and critiques the sixth argument based on the finitude of intervals between causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against infinite regress (based on the finitude of intervals between causes) and why al-Taftazani finds it problematic for formal debate.

Core Idea
The 'ma bayn' argument fails because the finitude of intervals is not granted in an infinite series.

Al-Taftazani now examines the sixth argument intended to disprove an infinite causal regress. This argument focuses on the interval (ma bayn) between an effect and each of its remote causes, claiming that such intervals must be finite and thus the whole series is finite. As you read, notice how the author quickly flags its limitations and then dismantles a related proof based on the composition of finite numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation once to grasp the flow. Notice that al-Taftazani first gives a brief version of the argument, then a more detailed one with the distance analogy, followed by his critique.
The argument is presented in two formulations; the second includes the term 'hads' (intuition).
2
Identify the core reasoning of the sixth argument
The argument claims that the interval between any effect and each remote cause is bounded by two boundaries (hasirin), hence finite. Therefore the entire chain cannot exceed the finite except by a small addition, so it must be finite.
This reasoning relies on an intuitive premise (hads) that the intervals are finite.
3
Understand the critique: reliance on intuition
Al-Taftazani notes that the argument works for reflection but not for formal debate because an opponent can deny the intuitive premise, arguing that in an infinite regress the intervals themselves are infinite, so the analogy to a finite distance fails.
This shows why the argument is not logically coercive.
4
Analyze the critique of the 'composite of finite numbers' argument
The passage also dismisses a separate attempt to prove finitude by stating that a composite of finite numbers is finite. Al-Taftazani calls this extremely weak because it merely restates the claim and assumes the number of numbers is finite, which is not granted.
He warns that this reasoning can lead to the fallacy that if each part is finite, the whole is finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : ما بين هذا المعلول وكل من علله البعيدة متناه لكونه بين حاصرين فتتناهى السلسلة لأنها حينئذ لا تزيد على المتناهي إلا بواحد ضرورة أنه إذا لم يزد ما بين هذه المسافة وكل جزء منه على فرسخ لم يزد الكل على فرسخ إلا بجزء بحكم الحدس وفيه نظر. وأما البيان بأن المتألف من الأعداد المتناهية لا يكون إلا متناهياً ففي غاية الضعف. الوجه السادس : إن ما بين هذا المعلول كالمعلول الأخير وكل من علله البعيدة الواقعة في السلسلة متناه ضرورة كونه محصوراً بين حاصرين، وهذا يستلزم تناهى السلسلة لأنها حينئذ لا تزيد على المتناهي إلا بواحد بحكم الحدس، فإنه إذا كان ما بين مبدأ المسافة وكل جزء من الأجزاء الواقعة فيها، لا يزيد على فرسخ فالمسافة لا تزيد على فرسخ لا بجزء هو المنتهى، إن جعلنا المبدأ مندرجاً على ما هو المفهوم من قولنا شيء خمسين إلى ستين، وإلا فجزأين فيصلح الدليل للنظر، وإصابة المطلوب وإن لم يصلح للمناظرة، وإلزام الخصم لأنه قد لا يذعن للمقدمة الحدسية، بل ربما يمنعها مستنداً بأنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان مراتب ما بين متناهية كما في المسافة وإما على تقدير لا تناهيها كما في السلسلة، فلا، إذ لا ينتهى إلى ما بين لا يوجد ما بين آخر أزيد منه، وقد تبين الاستلزام بأن المتألف من الأعداد المتناهية لا يكون إلا متناهياً وهو في غاية الضعف، لأنه إعادة للدعوى، بل ما هو أبعد منها وأخفى، لأن التألف من نفس الآحاد أقرب إلى التناهي من التألف الأعداد التي كل منها متناهية الآحاد، فالمنع عليه أظهر. وإنما يتم لو كانت عدة الأعداد المتناهية متناهية وهو غير لازم، ومن ههنا يذهب الوهم إلى أن هذا استدلال بثبوت الحكم، أعني التناهي لكل على ثبوته للكل وهو باطل.
The sixth argument: That between this effect and each of its remote causes is finite because it is between two boundaries, so the series ends because it then does not exceed the finite except by one, necessarily because if what is between this distance and every part of it does not exceed a farsakh, then the whole does not exceed a farsakh except by a part, by way of intuition (hads). But it is problematic. As for the demonstration that the composite of finite numbers is only finite, it is extremely weak. The sixth argument: That between this effect, such as the final effect, and each of its remote causes occurring in the series is finite due to its being between two boundaries, and this entails the finitude of the series because it then does not exceed the finite except by one, by way of intuition. For if what is between the beginning of the distance and every part of the parts occurring in it does not exceed a farsakh, then the distance does not exceed a farsakh except by a part, which is the endpoint, if we include the beginning as is understood from our saying 'something from fifty to sixty', otherwise by two parts. So the proof is valid for reflection and hitting the goal, even if not valid for debate and refuting the opponent, because he may not concede the intuitive premise, but rather may deny it on the grounds that this only follows if the ranks of 'what is between' are finite as in a distance, but on the assumption of their infinity as in the series, then not. For there is no end to what is between such that there is no 'what is between' beyond it greater than it. And the entailment has been shown by saying that the composite of finite numbers is only finite, but this is extremely weak because it is a repetition of the claim, indeed further and more hidden, because composition from simple units is closer to finitude than composition from numbers each of which has finite units, so the denial is more apparent. It only holds if the count of finite numbers is finite, which is not necessary. Hence the mind may go to the illusion that this is a proof by establishing the judgment, i.e., finitude, for each part to establish it for the whole, which is invalid.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents and critiques the sixth argument against infinite regress. The argument comes in two formulations: first a brief one, then a more detailed one. The core idea is that the interval between the final effect and any remote cause in a causal chain is bounded by two endpoints, hence finite. Since the whole series cannot exceed a finite by more than one (or a small number), the series must be finite. The author immediately notes that this argument is problematic ('fihi nazar'). He then dismisses another argument (the composition of finite numbers) as extremely weak. The detailed version of the sixth argument adds the analogy of a distance (masafa) measured in farsakhs: if each segment between the start and any point does not exceed a farsakh, then the whole distance does not exceed a farsakh except by a small part. The author notes that this proof works for reflection but not for formal debate because the opponent may reject the intuitive premise (hads) that the intervals are finite. The opponent might argue that in an infinite series, the 'between' intervals themselves are infinite, so the analogy to a finite distance fails. The author then analyzes the attempt to prove finitude by saying 'the composite of finite numbers is only finite'. He says this is weak because it merely restates the claim, and it is even weaker than the original point, since composing from units is more clearly finite than composing from numbers that are themselves finite. The proof would only work if the number of finite numbers being composed is itself finite, which is not granted. Finally, the author warns that this line of reasoning may lead to the fallacy of assuming that because each part has a property (finitude), the whole also has it, which is invalid. Thus the critique shows the limitations of such arguments.

Key Terms
ما بين
حاصرين
حدس
مسافة
فرسخ
تألف
أعداد
المتناهي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'ma bayn' argument is an airtight logical proof that an infinite regress is impossible.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that the argument relies on an intuitive premise (that the intervals are finite) which an opponent can deny in an infinite series, and thus it is not compelling in formal debate. Additionally, the related 'composite of finite numbers' argument is extremely weak and commits a fallacy.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani consider the 'ma bayn' argument problematic for formal debate?
A) Because it relies on an intuitive premise (hads) that the opponent may not accept.
B) Because it uses the concept of distance which is irrelevant.
C) Because it tries to prove the finitude of the series by assuming it is finite.
D) Because it is too complex to understand.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument against infinite regress is not conclusive because it relies on an unproven intuitive premise, and the alternative argument from the composition of finite numbers is tautological and commits a fallacy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you argue for your beliefs, do you sometimes rely on an intuition or assumption that you haven't critically examined? What would it take for you to test that intuition against a deeper analysis, as al-Taftazani does here?
?
The author warns that proving something for each part does not automatically prove it for the whole. In your spiritual life, where might you be assuming that a small success in one area guarantees overall success, while neglecting the bigger picture?
?
This passage shows that even a seemingly strong argument can be flawed if its premises are not granted. Is there an argument you hold about God or existence that you have not subjected to such scrutiny? What would it mean for you to honestly examine it, even if it challenges your comfort?

The Sixth Argument Against Infinite Regress and Its Weakness
Al-Taftazani presents and critiques the sixth argument against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth argument against infinite regress and why al-Taftazani considers it weak.

Core Idea
A conjectural premise cannot prove the finitude of an infinite chain.

Al-Taftazani presents the sixth of several arguments meant to refute infinite regress (tasalsul). He states the argument, which relies on a conjectural (hads) premise about the finitude of intermediate steps, then immediately critiques it as weak and circular. This lesson examines both the argument and the critique, showing why rational theologians must be careful about such proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the author's own presentation come through.
Notice the formal style of argumentation.
2
Identify the core claim
The argument states that the intermediate between an effect and its causes is finite because bounded, so the chain is finite. Find this claim in the text.
3
Understand the 'conjectural' label
Al-Taftazani says the proof is based on conjecture (hads) and not compelling to an opponent. Why might an opponent reject the premise?
The opponent could deny that the intermediates are finite.
4
Analyze the critique
The author says the reasoning that a composite of finite numbers is finite is merely a restatement of the claim and even more obscure. Trace this in the text.
He calls it 'extremely weak' and 'repetition of the claim'.
5
Reflect on the fallacy
The argument moves from finitude of each part to finitude of the whole, which is invalid. Consider why this is problematic.
This fallacy is highlighted at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السادس : إن ما بين هذا المعلول كالمعلول الأخير وكل من علله البعيدة الواقعة في السلسلة متناه ضرورة كونه محصوراً بين حاصرين، وهذا يستلزم تناهى السلسلة لأنها حينئذ لا تزيد على المتناهي إلا بواحد بحكم الحدس، فإنه إذا كان ما بين مبدأ المسافة وكل جزء من الأجزاء الواقعة فيها، لا يزيد على فرسخ فالمسافة لا تزيد على فرسخ لا بجزء هو المنتهى، إن جعلنا المبدأ مندرجاً على ما هو المفهوم من قولنا شيء خمسين إلى ستين، وإلا فجزأين فيصلح الدليل للنظر، وإصابة المطلوب وإن لم يصلح للمناظرة، وإلزام الخصم لأنه قد لا يذعن للمقدمة الحدسية، بل ربما يمنعها مستنداً بأنه إنما يلزم ذلك لو كان مراتب ما بين متناهية كما في المسافة وإما على تقدير لا تناهيها كما في السلسلة، فلا، إذ لا ينتهى إلى ما بين لا يوجد ما بين آخر أزيد منه، وقد تبين الاستلزام بأن المتألف من الأعداد المتناهية لا يكون إلا متناهياً وهو في غاية الضعف، لأنه إعادة للدعوى، بل ما هو أبعد منها وأخفى، لأن التألف من نفس الآحاد أقرب إلى التناهي من التألف الأعداد التي كل منها متناهية الآحاد ، فالمنع عليه أظهر. وإنما يتم لو كانت عدة الأعداد المتناهية متناهية وهو غير لازم ، ومن ههنا يذهب الوهم إلى أن هذا استدلال بثبوت الحكم، أعني التناهي لكل على ثبوته للكل وهو باطل
The sixth way: That which is between this effect, like the last effect, and each of its remote causes occurring in the chain, is finite necessarily because it is confined between two containments, and this entails the finitude of the chain, because then it does not exceed the finite except by one, by conjecture. For if that which is between the beginning of the distance and each part of the parts occurring in it does not exceed a parasang, then the distance does not exceed a parasang not by a part which is the end, if we consider the beginning included as understood from our saying 'something from fifty to sixty', otherwise by two parts. So the proof is valid for theoretical investigation and hitting the target, even if not valid for disputation and compelling the opponent, because he may not assent to the conjectural premise, but rather may deny it on the ground that this only follows if the degrees of what is between are finite, as in the case of distance; but on the assumption of their infinitude, as in the chain, then no, because it does not end at what is beyond which there is no [further] beyond greater than it. And the entailment has been shown in that the composite of finite numbers is only finite, but this is extremely weak, because it is a restatement of the claim, indeed something more remote and obscure, because composition from the units themselves is closer to finitude than composition from numbers each of which has finite units. So denial of it is more apparent. It would only be valid if the number of finite numbers were finite, which is not necessary. And from here misunderstanding goes to the idea that this is arguing from the establishment of the judgment (i.e., finitude) for each individual to its establishment for the whole, which is false.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage presents and then critiques the sixth argument against infinite regress (tasalsul). The argument begins by stating that the intermediate between an effect and its remote causes in a chain is finite because it is bounded between two limits. Therefore, the whole chain is only one more than that finite intermediate, so it must be finite. The author immediately notes that this argument relies on a conjectural (hads) premise, which an opponent may reject. He explains that the premise is only plausible if the degrees of the intermediate are finite, like in a spatial distance, but if the chain is infinite, the intermediate may not have a maximum beyond which there is no more. Then he critiques the underlying principle that a composite of finite numbers is finite, calling it extremely weak because it merely restates the claim and is even more obscure, as composition from units is more obviously finite than composition from numbers that are themselves composed of finite units. The argument would only be valid if the number of finite numbers were itself finite, which is not given. He concludes that the reasoning wrongly moves from finitude of each part to finitude of the whole, which is a fallacy. Thus, the sixth way is not a strong proof.

Key Terms
تسلسل
حدس
متناه
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sixth argument is a strong proof against infinite regress.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows it relies on a conjectural premise and merely restates the claim, making it weak and circular.

Checkpoint
Why does al-Taftazani consider the sixth argument weak?
A) It relies on a conjectural premise and merely restates the claim.
B) It contradicts the Quran.
C) It is too long and complex.
D) It only applies to spatial distances.

Key Takeaway
The sixth argument fails because its premise is conjectural and its reasoning is circular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on an argument that seemed convincing but was actually weak, simply because it supported your position? Are you willing to abandon such arguments when they are shown to be unsound, even if it means your position seems less secure?
?
The author critiques even his own tradition's arguments for being conjectural. Is there a cherished belief you hold that you have not subjected to the same rigorous scrutiny, because you fear what you might find?
?
In your daily life, where might you be committing the fallacy of assuming that because each individual part is fine, the whole must be fine (e.g., habits, finances, relationships)? What will you do to correct this?

The Seventh Argument Against Infinite Regress and Its Key Objection
Taftazani's seventh argument and its objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof against infinite regress based on counting thousands, and the crucial objection that equality and inequality do not apply to infinite magnitudes.

Core Idea
An infinite chain cannot have a number of groups less than its units—so it must be finite.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a sophisticated mathematical argument against infinite regress. He attempts to show that even considering groups of units ('thousands') leads to finitude. But he also records an objection that challenges the very foundation of such counting arguments. Understanding this objection is key to appreciating the limits of rational proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without overanalyzing. Let the logical flow settle in your mind.
The passage contains a dense argument followed by an objection.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The argument starts by considering the number of thousands relative to units: equal, more, or less. Find these three cases in the text.
Equality and more are dismissed as impossible.
3
Follow the derivation for 'less'
Trace the reasoning when the number of thousands is less. The units are divided into two sums. See how the argument shows finitude from both the finite and infinite sides.
This step requires careful attention to the logical moves.
4
Understand the objection
The passage then notes a fundamental objection: equality and inequality may not apply to infinite quantities. Reflect on why this undermines the argument.
This objection also applies to earlier proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع من إبطال التسلسل (قال : السابع : عدة ألوف السلسلة إما مساوية بعدة آحادها أو أكثر وهو ظاهر الاستحالة أو أقل فيشمل الآحاد على جملة بقدر عدة الألوف، وأخرى بقدر الزائد والأولى إن كانت من الجانب المتناهي حقيقة أو فرضاً تتناهى عدة الألوف ضرورة وجود مقطع يكون مبدأ للزائد وحينئذ تتناهى السلسلة . لتألفها من جمل متناهية الأعداد والأحاد، وإن كانت من الجانب الغير المتناهي وقعت الثانية من الجانب المتناهي ما بين الطرف، ومبدأ عدة الألوف فتكون متناهية وهى فضل آحاد السلسلة على عدة الألوف، فتتناهى عدة الألوف والسلسلة بالضرورة، ويرد عليه وعلى بعض ما سبق منع لزوم التساوي والتفاوت فى غير المتناهي ومنع لزوم انقطاع الأقل فيه . الوجه السابع : أنه لو وجدت سلسلة بل جملة غير متناهية سواء كانت من العلل والمعلولات أو غيرهما مجتمعة أو متعاقبة فهى لا محالة تشتمل على
The seventh way of refuting infinite regress. He said: The seventh: The number of thousands of the chain is either equal to the number of its units, or more — which is clearly impossible — or less. Then the units include a sum equal to the number of the thousands, and another sum equal to the excess. The first sum, if it is from the finite side (in reality or by hypothesis), the number of thousands becomes finite necessarily because there is a cut that is the beginning of the excess, and then the chain becomes finite, because it is composed of sums with finite numbers and units. And if it is from the infinite side, the second sum falls from the finite side between the end and the beginning of the number of thousands, so it becomes finite, and it is the excess of the units of the chain over the number of thousands. Hence the number of thousands and the chain are necessarily finite. But it and some of what preceded are objected to by denying the necessity of equality and inequality in the infinite, and denying the necessity of termination of the lesser in it. The seventh way: If a chain, indeed a series, were infinite, whether of causes and effects or other, simultaneous or successive, it inevitably contains...
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument against infinite regress (tasalsul) and then states an objection to it. The argument considers the number of thousands (groups of units) in an infinite chain. It posits three possibilities: the number of thousands equals the number of units, is more, or is less. Equality and more are deemed impossible because a group cannot have as many or more groups than its members. So it must be less. Then the units are divided into two sums: one equal to the number of thousands, and another equal to the excess. If the first sum is from the finite side (i.e., the part of the chain that is finite), then the number of thousands is finite, and the whole chain is finite. If the first sum is from the infinite side, then the second sum (the excess) falls within a finite segment between the end of the finite side and the beginning of the number of thousands, making it finite. This excess, added to the number of thousands, would make the whole finite. The argument concludes necessarily that the chain is finite. The author then notes an objection (يرد عليه) that applies to this and some earlier proofs: that equality and inequality do not apply to infinite magnitudes, and that a lesser part does not necessarily terminate within an infinite whole. The passage then begins to restate the seventh way, possibly to address the objection, but is cut off. Thus, the lesson covers the argument and the objection without a reply.

Key Terms

عدة ألوف
تساوي
تفاوت
غير متناه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The seventh argument is an airtight proof that infinite regress is impossible.
Correction: The argument relies on the applicability of equality and inequality to infinite quantities, which is contested by the objection.

Checkpoint
What is the main objection to the seventh argument?
A) Equality and inequality do not apply to infinite magnitudes.
B) Thousands cannot be counted in an infinite series.
C) The chain must be finite by definition.
D) The argument only works for simultaneous series.

Key Takeaway
The seventh argument attempts to prove finiteness via counting groups, but the objection reveals the limits of such numerical reasoning with infinities.

The Seventh Proof: Thousands Force Finitude
Taftazani's seventh proof using thousands and units to refute infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani's seventh proof uses the relationship between the number of thousands and the number of units in an infinite series to demonstrate that the series must be finite.

Core Idea
Comparing thousands to units forces an infinite series to become finite.

Taftazani presents a clever argument that exploits the mismatch between the count of groups (thousands) and the count of individual items (units) in an infinite series. By considering whether these counts can be equal, greater, or less, he reduces the assumption of infinity to absurdity. This lesson walks through the logical steps of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Setup
Assume an infinite series of causes or effects. It contains thousands of units. Consider the count of thousands and the count of units as numbers. There are three possibilities: equal, greater, or less.
The proof treats the series as a collection of units grouped into thousands.
2
Eliminate Equal and Greater
Equal is impossible because if each thousand corresponded to one unit (to count thousands), the units would be a thousand times the count of thousands. Greater is also impossible for the same reason.
3
Examine the Less Case
If the count of thousands is less than the count of units, then the units split into two groups: one equal to the count of thousands, and the surplus. The group equal to the thousands is either from the finite side or the infinite side. Both lead to the finitude of the thousands and thus the whole series.
This is the core of the reductio.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه السابع من إبطال التسلسل. قال: السابع: عدة ألوف السلسلة إما مساوية بعدة آحادها أو أكثر وهو ظاهر الاستحالة أو أقل فيشمل الآحاد على جملة بقدر عدة الألوف، وأخرى بقدر الزائد والأولى إن كانت من الجانب المتناهي حقيقة أو فرضاً تتناهى عدة الألوف ضرورة وجود مقطع يكون مبدأ للزائد وحينئذ تتناهى السلسلة لتألفها من جمل متناهية الأعداد والأحاد، وإن كانت من الجانب الغير المتناهي وقعت الثانية من الجانب المتناهي ما بين الطرف ومبدأ عدة الألوف فتكون متناهية وهى فضل آحاد السلسلة على عدة الألوف، فتتناهى عدة الألوف والسلسلة بالضرورة. ثم قال: الوجه السابع: أنه لو وجدت سلسلة بل جملة غير متناهية سواء كانت من العلل والمعلولات أو غيرهما مجتمعة أو متعاقبة فهي لا محالة تشتمل على ألوف، فعدة الألوف الموجودة فيها، إما أن تكون مساوية لعدة أحادها أو أكثر وهو ظاهر الاستحالة، لأن هذه الآحاد يجب أن تكون ألف مرة مثل عدة الألوف، لأن معناها: أن يأخذ كل ألف من الآحاد واحداً حتى يكون عدة مائة ألف مائة، وإما أن يكون أقل وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الأحاد حينئذ تشتمل على جملتين: إحداهما: بقدر عدة الألوف والأخرى بقدر الزائد عليها، والأولى أعني الجملة التي بقدر عدة الألوف، إما أن تكون من الجانب المتناهي أو الجانب الغير المتناهي، وعلى التقديرين يلزم تناهي السلسلة، هذا خلف. وإن كانت السلسلة غير متناهية من الجانبين يفرض مقطعاً فيحصل جانب متناهي، فيتأتى الترديد، أما لزوم التناهي على التقدير الأول، فلأن عدة الألوف متناهية، لكونها محصورة بين حاصرين هما طرف السلسلة والمقطع الذي هو مبدأ الجملة الثانية أعنى الزائد على عدة الألوف على ما هو المفروض، وإذا تناهت عدة الألوف تناهت السلسلة لكونها عبارة عن مجموع الأحاد المتألفة من تلك العدة من الألوف، والمتألف من الجمل المتناهية الأعداد والآحاد متناه بالضرورة. وأما على التقدير الثاني فلأن الجملة التي بقدر الزائد على عدة الألوف تقع في الجانب المتناهي وتكون متناهية ضرورة انحصارها بين طرف السلسلة ومبدأ عدة الألوف، وهى أضعاف عدة الألوف بتسعمائة وتسعة وتسعين مرة فيلزم تناهي عدة الألوف بالضرورة، ويلزم تناهي السلسلة لتناهي أجزائها عدة وأحاداً على ما مر. ويرد عليه وعلى بعض ما سبق منع لزوم التساوي والتفاوت في غير المتناهي ومنع لزوم انقطاع الأقل فيه.
The seventh aspect of refuting infinite regress. He said: The seventh: The count of thousands of the series is either equal to the count of its units or greater—which is manifestly impossible—or less, such that the units include a group equal to the count of thousands and another [group] equal to the surplus. The first, if it is from the finite side, truly or hypothetically, the count of thousands becomes finite necessarily because of the existence of a cut that is the beginning of the surplus, and then the series becomes finite because it is composed of groups with finite numbers and units. And if it is from the infinite side, the second [group] falls on the finite side between the endpoint and the beginning of the count of thousands, so it is finite, and it is the excess of the units of the series over the count of thousands. Thus the count of thousands and the series become finite necessarily. Then he said: The seventh aspect: If a series—rather an infinite aggregate—exists, whether of causes and effects or other things, simultaneous or successive, it inevitably contains thousands. The count of thousands existing in it is either equal to the count of its units or greater—which is manifestly impossible because these units must be a thousand times the count of thousands (since its meaning is that each thousand of the units takes one until the count of a hundred thousand becomes a hundred)—or it is less, which is also invalid because the units then include two groups: one equal to the count of thousands and the other equal to the surplus. The first, namely the group equal to the count of thousands, either is from the finite side or the infinite side. On both hypotheses, the finitude of the series follows—this is a reductio. If the series is infinite from both sides, a cut is assumed, so a finite side is obtained; then the disjunction is applicable. As for the necessary finitude on the first hypothesis, it is because the count of thousands is finite, being enclosed between two boundaries: the endpoint of the series and the cut which is the beginning of the second group, namely the surplus over the count of thousands as supposed. When the count of thousands is finite, the series becomes finite because it is the aggregate of the units composed of that count of thousands, and what is composed of groups with finite numbers and units is necessarily finite. As for the second hypothesis, it is because the group equal to the surplus over the count of thousands falls on the finite side and is finite necessarily due to its confinement between the endpoint of the series and the beginning of the count of thousands, and it is nine hundred and ninety-nine times the count of thousands; thus the finitude of the count of thousands follows necessarily, and the finitude of the series follows from the finitude of its parts in count and units as previously. And an objection is raised against this and against some of what preceded: the denial of the necessity of equality and inequality in the infinite and the denial of the necessity of the interruption of the lesser in it.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof against infinite regress (tasalsul) and then records an objection to it. The proof is based on the relationship between the number of thousands in an infinite series and the number of units composing those thousands. The argument assumes an infinite series (of causes and effects or any other items) and proceeds by considering the count of thousands it contains. There are three possibilities: the count of thousands equals the count of units, exceeds it, or is less. Equality is impossible because if each thousand corresponds to one unit (to count the thousands), the units would be a thousand times the thousands, not equal. Excess is likewise impossible. So it must be less. That means the units can be divided into two groups: one equal to the count of thousands and another (the surplus). Now the first group (equal to the thousands) is either from a finite side or from an infinite side. If it is from a finite side, then the count of thousands is finite because it is bounded by the endpoint of the series and a cut (the beginning of the surplus). If it is from an infinite side, then the second group (the surplus) lies on the finite side between the endpoint and the beginning of the thousands, so it is finite. But this surplus is 999 times the count of thousands, so the count of thousands must also be finite. Either way, the count of thousands is finite, and since the series is composed of a finite number of thousands each containing a finite number of units, the entire series is finite. This contradicts the assumption of infinity. The objector, however, denies a key premise: that the relations of equality, greater, and less apply to infinite numbers. The objector also denies that a lesser infinite must reach an end (interruption). This objection challenges the proof's applicability to truly infinite quantities.

Key Takeaway
By analyzing the relationship between thousands and units, Taftazani shows that an infinite series cannot exist; it must be finite.

?
This proof shows that even if you start from the infinite side, there is always a finite 'surplus' that forces finitude. In your life, are you living as if your mistakes can accumulate infinitely, without a final cut (muqtaṭaʿ)? When will you make that decisive break from a sin you've been repeating, knowing that the series of repents must eventually become finite?

When you do a good deed, do you attach it to the finite series of this world, or do you cut it to the Infinite One? Which 'thousand' are you counting on: the number of your good deeds or the mercy of the One who cuts the series?

Objection to the Proof: Comparing Infinities
Taftazani records an objection against the seventh proof and previous ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection raised against Taftazani's seventh proof: that relations of equality, greater, and less do not apply to infinite quantities, and that a lesser infinite does not necessarily terminate.

Core Idea
Infinite quantities defy comparison as equal, greater, or less.

After presenting the seventh proof, Taftazani records an important objection that challenges a foundational assumption: that infinite sets can be compared in terms of count. The objector denies that relations like equality and inequality apply to infinities, and also denies that a lesser infinite must have an end. This lesson explores the objection and its implications for the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Objection
Read the short objection passage carefully. Identify the two denials: (1) that equality/inequality apply to infinite quantities, and (2) that a lesser infinite must cut off.
2
Understand the First Denial
The objector says: the relations of equality, greater, and less do not apply to infinite quantities. This means the entire classification in the proof is invalid because those categories are meaningless for infinities.
This is a foundational critique: infinite sets are not like finite sets in terms of cardinality comparisons.
3
Understand the Second Denial
Even if we allow comparison, the objector denies that a lesser infinite must reach an end. In the proof, the assumption that the lesser group (the thousands) must be bounded by a cut is challenged.
4
Reflect on the Implications
Consider how this objection undermines the proof. Does it save the possibility of an actual infinite? The objector is not necessarily proving the existence of an infinite regress, but showing that the proof is not conclusive.

The seventh aspect of refuting infinite regress. He said: The seventh: The count of thousands of the series is either equal to the count of its units or greater—which is manifestly impossible—or less, such that the units include a group equal to the count of thousands and another [group] equal to the surplus. The first, if it is from the finite side, truly or hypothetically, the count of thousands becomes finite necessarily because of the existence of a cut that is the beginning of the surplus, and then the series becomes finite because it is composed of groups with finite numbers and units. And if it is from the infinite side, the second [group] falls on the finite side between the endpoint and the beginning of the count of thousands, so it is finite, and it is the excess of the units of the series over the count of thousands. Thus the count of thousands and the series become finite necessarily. Then he said: The seventh aspect: If a series—rather an infinite aggregate—exists, whether of causes and effects or other things, simultaneous or successive, it inevitably contains thousands. The count of thousands existing in it is either equal to the count of its units or greater—which is manifestly impossible because these units must be a thousand times the count of thousands (since its meaning is that each thousand of the units takes one until the count of a hundred thousand becomes a hundred)—or it is less, which is also invalid because the units then include two groups: one equal to the count of thousands and the other equal to the surplus. The first, namely the group equal to the count of thousands, either is from the finite side or the infinite side. On both hypotheses, the finitude of the series follows—this is a reductio. If the series is infinite from both sides, a cut is assumed, so a finite side is obtained; then the disjunction is applicable. As for the necessary finitude on the first hypothesis, it is because the count of thousands is finite, being enclosed between two boundaries: the endpoint of the series and the cut which is the beginning of the second group, namely the surplus over the count of thousands as supposed. When the count of thousands is finite, the series becomes finite because it is the aggregate of the units composed of that count of thousands, and what is composed of groups with finite numbers and units is necessarily finite. As for the second hypothesis, it is because the group equal to the surplus over the count of thousands falls on the finite side and is finite necessarily due to its confinement between the endpoint of the series and the beginning of the count of thousands, and it is nine hundred and ninety-nine times the count of thousands; thus the finitude of the count of thousands follows necessarily, and the finitude of the series follows from the finitude of its parts in count and units as previously. And an objection is raised against this and against some of what preceded: the denial of the necessity of equality and inequality in the infinite and the denial of the necessity of the interruption of the lesser in it.

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh proof against infinite regress (tasalsul) and then records an objection to it. The proof is based on the relationship between the number of thousands in an infinite series and the number of units composing those thousands. The argument assumes an infinite series (of causes and effects or any other items) and proceeds by considering the count of thousands it contains. There are three possibilities: the count of thousands equals the count of units, exceeds it, or is less. Equality is impossible because if each thousand corresponds to one unit (to count the thousands), the units would be a thousand times the thousands, not equal. Excess is likewise impossible. So it must be less. That means the units can be divided into two groups: one equal to the count of thousands and another (the surplus). Now the first group (equal to the thousands) is either from a finite side or from an infinite side. If it is from a finite side, then the count of thousands is finite because it is bounded by the endpoint of the series and a cut (the beginning of the surplus). If it is from an infinite side, then the second group (the surplus) lies on the finite side between the endpoint and the beginning of the thousands, so it is finite. But this surplus is 999 times the count of thousands, so the count of thousands must also be finite. Either way, the count of thousands is finite, and since the series is composed of a finite number of thousands each containing a finite number of units, the entire series is finite. This contradicts the assumption of infinity. The objector, however, denies a key premise: that the relations of equality, greater, and less apply to infinite numbers. The objector also denies that a lesser infinite must reach an end (interruption). This objection challenges the proof's applicability to truly infinite quantities.

Proof of the Finitude of a Series
The author proves the finitude of a series using a mathematical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that an infinite series of causes and effects leads to a logical contradiction, using a mathematical analogy of thousands and units.

Core Idea
An infinite causal series is impossible; it must be finite.

This passage presents a core argument in kalām against an infinite regress of causes. The author uses a mathematical example involving thousands and units to show that assuming an infinite series leads to logical absurdities. Let's walk through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of disjunctive propositions (either equal, greater, or less).
2
Identify the two main branches
The author presents two possibilities: either the number of thousands equals or exceeds the number of units, or it is less. Find each and note how he dismisses the first as impossible.
The first branch is dismissed quickly; the second is analyzed in detail.
3
Follow the analysis of the second branch
When the number of thousands is less, the units split into two sums. The first sum must come from either the finite side or the infinite side. Trace the reasoning for each subcase.
Both subcases lead to the conclusion that the series is finite.
4
Understand the overall conclusion
The author concludes that in all scenarios the series must be finite, contradicting the assumption of an infinite series. This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
The argument relies on the idea that if a part is finite, the whole composed of such parts is finite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألوف، فعدة الألوف الموجودة فيها، إما أن تكون (۱) مساوية لعدة أحادها أو أكثر وهو ظاهر الاستحالة ، لأن هذه الآحاد يجب أن تكون ألف مرة مثل عدة الألوف ، لأن معناها : أن يأخذ كل ألف من الآحاد واجداً حتى يكون عدة مائة ألف مائة، وإما أن يكون ) أقل وهو أيضاً باطل، لأن الأحاد حينئذ تشتمل على جملتين : إحداهما : بقدر عدة الألوف والأخرى بقدر الزائد عليها، والأولى أعني الجملة التي بقدر عدة الألوف، إما أن تكون (۲) من الجانب المتناهي أو الجانب الغير المتناهي، وعلى التقديرين يلزم تناهي السلسلة ، هذا خلف (۳). وإن كانت السلسلة غير متناهية من الجانبين يفرض مقطعاً فيحصل جانب متناهي، فيتأتى الترديد، أما لزوم التناهي على التقدير الأول، فلأن عدة الألوف متناهية، لكونها محصورة بين حاصرين هما (٤) طرف السلسلة والمقطع الذي هو مبدأ (٥) الجملة الثانية أعنى الزائد على عدة الألوف على ما هو المفروض، وإذا تناهت عدة الألوف تناهت السلسلة لكونها عبارة عن مجموع الأحاد المتألفة من تلك العدة من الألوف ، والمتألف من الجمل المتناهية الأعداد والآحاد متناه بالضرورة. وأما على التقدير الثاني فلأن الجملة التي بقدر الزائد على عدة الألوف تقع في الجانب المتناهي وتكون متناهية ضرورة انحصارها بين طرف السلسلة ومبدأ عدة الألوف، وهى أضعاف عدة (٦) الألوف بتسعمائة وتسعة وتسعين مرة فيلزم تناهي عدة الألوف بالضرورة ، ويلزم (۷) تناهي السلسلة لتناهي أجزائها عدة وأحاداً على ما مر، ويرد عليه وعلى بعض ما سبق منع المنفصلة القائلة (۱۰) بأن هذا مساو لذاك أو أكثر أو أقل، فإن التساوى والتفاوت من خواص المتناهي وإن أريد بالتساوى مجرد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء من هذا جزء من ذاك، فلا نسلم استحالته فيما بين العدتين كما في الواحد إلى مالا يتناهى والعشرة إلى ما لا يتناهى وكون أحدهما أضعاف (٢) الآخر لا ينافي التساوى بهذا المعنى ولو سلم. فمنع كون الأقل منقطعاً، فإن السلسلة إذا كانت غير متناهية كان بعضها الذى من الجانب الغير المتناهي أيضاً غير متناه وكذا عدة ألوفها أو مئاتها أو عشراتها، وحديث الجملتين، وانقطاع أولاهما بمبدأ الثانية كاذب .
Thousands: the number of thousands present in it is either (1) equal to the number of their units or greater, and that is clearly impossible, because these units must be a thousand times the number of thousands, since its meaning is that each thousand of the units takes one until the number of a hundred thousand becomes a hundred; or it is less, and that is also false, because then the units comprise two sums: one equal to the number of thousands and the other equal to the excess over it. The first, meaning the sum equal to the number of thousands, is either (2) from the finite side or the infinite side; and on both assumptions the finitude of the series follows, which is absurd (3). If the series is infinite from both sides, a cut is assumed, so a finite side results, and the division can be applied. As for the necessity of finitude on the first assumption: because the number of thousands is finite, being confined between two boundaries (4), which are the end of the series and the cut which is the beginning (5) of the second sum, i.e., the excess over the number of thousands as assumed. And if the number of thousands is finite, the series is finite because it is the sum of the units composed of that number of thousands, and what is composed of sums finite in number and units is necessarily finite. As for the second assumption: because the sum equal to the excess over the number of thousands falls on the finite side and is necessarily finite due to its being confined between the end of the series and the beginning of the number of thousands, and it is nine hundred and ninety-nine times the number of thousands; thus the finitude of the number of thousands necessarily follows, and the finitude of the series follows due to the finitude of its parts in number and units, as previously mentioned. An objection is raised against this and against some of what preceded: the denial of the disjunctive (10) saying that this is equal to that, or greater, or less. For equality and inequality are properties of the finite. But if what is meant by equality is merely that over against every part of this there is a part of that, then we do not concede its impossibility between the two counts, as in one to infinity and ten to infinity, and one being a multiple (2) of the other does not negate equality in this sense. Even if this is conceded, then the claim that the smaller is cut off is denied; for if the series is infinite, a part of it from the infinite side is also infinite, and likewise the number of its thousands or hundreds or tens, and the talk of two sums and the cutting off of the first at the beginning of the second is false.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the finitude of a series (silsila) followed by the refutation of an objection. The author begins by presenting a mathematical analogy involving thousands (aluf) and units (ahad). He considers two possibilities: either the number of thousands equals or exceeds the number of units, which he dismisses as clearly impossible because the units must be a thousand times the number of thousands to make up the total; this would require the number of units to be at least a thousand times the number of thousands, leading to a contradiction. The second possibility is that the number of thousands is less than the number of units. He argues that this is also false because then the units can be divided into two sums: one equal to the number of thousands, and the other being the excess. The first sum (equal to the number of thousands) must either be taken from the finite side or the infinite side of the series. If it is from the finite side, then the number of thousands is finite because it is bounded between the end of the series and the beginning of the second sum; thus the whole series is finite. If it is from the infinite side, then the second sum (the excess) falls on the finite side and is finite, and since it is 999 times the number of thousands, the number of thousands must also be finite, again making the series finite. Thus in all cases the series is finite, which contradicts the assumption of an infinite series. The author then presents an objection. The objector denies the validity of the disjunctive assumption (the 'either equal, greater, or less') because equality and inequality are properties of finite quantities. If by 'equality' is meant merely a one-to-one correspondence, then it is possible even between infinite sets, as in the case of the natural numbers and their multiples (e.g., the series of ones compared to tens) — one being a multiple of the other does not prevent a one-to-one correspondence. The author responds by conceding the possibility of equality in that sense, but then denies the objector's claim that the smaller (i.e., the part from the infinite side) is cut off (finite). In an infinite series, any part taken from the infinite side is itself infinite, and likewise the number of thousands, hundreds, or tens within that part is infinite. Therefore the objection's assumption of two sums with the first cut off at the beginning of the second is false.

Key Terms
سلسلة (silsila, series)
ألوف (aluf, thousands)
آحاد (ahad, units)
متناهي (mutanahi, finite)
غير متناهي (ghayr mutanahi, infinite)
مقطع (maqta', cut)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude if the number of thousands is less than the number of units?
A) The series must be infinite.
B) The series must be finite.
C) The series is impossible.
D) The series is uncountable.

Key Takeaway
An infinite series of causes leads to a logical contradiction; therefore, any chain of contingent beings must terminate in a necessary being.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what areas of your life do you rely on an ‘infinite regress’ of excuses or dependencies rather than facing the ultimate foundation of your actions? What would it mean for you to stop that chain today?
?
If every chain of causes must end in a Necessary Being, what does that demand of you in terms of your trust and reliance on that Being versus on intermediate causes?
?
The author uses a logical proof to show that an infinite series is impossible. Is there a habit or pattern in your life that you have assumed is endless but, upon reflection, you know must be finite? How will you confront its limit?

Refutation of the Objection against the Finitude Argument
The author refutes an objection against the finitude proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that equality and inequality are properties of finite quantities, and defends the proof against an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Equality can hold between infinite sets by one-to-one correspondence.

After presenting the proof, the author anticipates an objection: the disjunctive reasoning assumes equality/inequality only applies to finite quantities. The author responds by clarifying the meaning of equality and then denying the objector's claim about the 'cut-off' point. Let's examine this dialectical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the objection (the disjunctive proposition is denied) and the author's two-part response: a concession with redefinition, then a denial of the cutoff.
Note the footnote marker (10) indicating the disjunctive proposition.
2
Understand the author's concession
The author concedes that if 'equality' means one-to-one correspondence, then it is possible between infinite sets (e.g., natural numbers and their multiples). This defuses part of the objection.
The author emphasizes that one being a multiple of the other does not negate equality in this sense.
3
Examine the author's denial of the cutoff
Even if equality is conceded, the objector claims the smaller part (from the infinite side) is cut off (finite). The author denies this: in an infinite series, any part from the infinite side is itself infinite, and so are its thousands, hundreds, tens, etc. Thus the talk of two sums with a cutoff is false.
This shows that the objection fails to undermine the original proof.

Correction: The author shows that equality can be understood as one-to-one correspondence, which applies to infinite sets, and that the objector's further claim about a 'cutoff' is false in an infinite series.

The objection based on the finite nature of equality is refuted by redefining equality as one-to-one correspondence, which holds even for infinite sets, and by denying that any part of an infinite series is finite.

Refuting Equality/Inequality in Infinite Series
The author denies the disjunctive proposition about equality or inequality between two infinite series.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the disjunctive proposition claiming equality or inequality between two infinite series is invalid, and how infinite series can be compared through one-to-one correspondence without contradiction.

Core Idea
Equality and inequality apply only to finite quantities.

This lesson examines a key objection against arguments involving infinite series. The author refutes the claim that two infinite series must be either equal or unequal in the finite sense, and instead shows how one-to-one correspondence can be used without contradiction, preserving consistency in discussions of infinity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive proposition
Read the passage and find the disjunctive proposition that the author is refuting: that one infinite series is equal to, greater than, or less than another.
The proposition is stated at the beginning: 'this is equal to that or more or less.'
2
Understand why equality is a property of the finite
The author states that equality and difference (inequality) are properties of the finite. Reflect on why this is a crucial principle in dealing with infinities.
Finite quantities can be compared by magnitude; infinite quantities cannot.
3
Learn the alternative definition of equality
The author introduces a one-to-one correspondence definition: 'opposite every part of this there is a part of that.' Notice that this does not involve magnitude and is possible for infinite sets.
Example: one-to-one between numbers 1,2,3... and 10,20,30...
4
See why the smaller series cannot be finite
The objector claims the smaller series must be finite, but the author argues that if the series is infinite, any part from the infinite side is also infinite.
Thus, the 'smaller' infinite series is still infinite, not cut off.
5
Recognize the refutation as complete
The passage ends by calling the objection 'false' (كاذب). Understand that the argument successfully defends the consistency of infinite series comparisons.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما سبق منع المنفصلة القائلة (١) بأن هذا مساو لذاك أو أكثر أو أقل، فإن التساوى والتفاوت من خواص المتناهي وإن أريد بالتساوى مجرد أن يقع بإزاء كل جزء من هذا جزء من ذاك، فلا نسلم استحالته فيما بين العدتين كما في الواحد إلى مالا يتناهى والعشرة إلى ما لا يتناهى وكون أحدهما أضعاف (٢) الآخر لا ينافي التساوى بهذا المعنى ولو سلم. فمنع كون الأقل منقطعاً، فإن السلسلة إذا كانت غير متناهية كان بعضها الذى من الجانب الغير المتناهي أيضاً غير متناه وكذا عدة ألوفها أو مئاتها أو عشراتها، وحديث الجملتين، وانقطاع أولاهما بمبدأ الثانية كاذب .
What preceded prevented the disjunctive proposition saying that this is equal to that or more or less. For equality and difference are properties of the finite. If by equality is meant merely that opposite every part of this there is a part of that, then we do not admit its impossibility between two sets, as in the one to infinity and the ten to infinity. And the fact that one is a multiple of the other does not negate equality in this sense. And if it is granted, then he prevents the smaller from being finite. For if the series is infinite, then a part of it from the infinite side is also infinite, and likewise the number of its thousands, hundreds, or tens. And the talk of the two sequences, and the first being cut off at the beginning of the second, is false.
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection concerning the comparison of two infinite series. The author begins by stating that the preceding discussion already prevented the disjunctive proposition that one infinite series is equal to, greater than, or less than another. He explains that equality and difference (inequality) are properties that belong only to finite things. If one redefines equality as a one-to-one correspondence (that opposite every part of one there is a part of the other), then such equality is not impossible between two infinite sets. He gives the example of the series from one to infinity and from ten to infinity: even if one is a multiple of the other, this does not violate equality in this sense. Even if this redefinition is granted, the objector still claims that the smaller series must be finite (cut off). The author denies this, arguing that if the series is infinite, then any part of it taken from the infinite side is also infinite. This applies to the number of thousands, hundreds, or tens as well. Thus, the claim that one series is cut off at the beginning of the other is false.

Key Terms
المنفصلة (disjunctive proposition)
التساوى (equality)
التفاوت (difference/inequality)
المتناهي (finite)
غير المتناهي (infinite)
السلسلة (series)
العدتين (two sets)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that equality and inequality apply to infinite sets as they do to finite ones.
Correction: The author shows that equality and inequality are properties of the finite; for infinite sets, one-to-one correspondence can be used without implying magnitude comparison.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the disjunctive proposition about equality or inequality between two infinite series prevented?
A) Because infinite series are never equal or unequal.
B) Because equality and inequality are properties of the finite.
C) Because the smaller series must always be finite.
D) Because one-to-one correspondence is impossible between infinite series.

Key Takeaway
Infinite series can be compared through one-to-one correspondence without falling into the false dichotomy of equality or inequality in the finite sense.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often compare your deeds to others, feeling superior or inferior. But this lesson says equality and inequality are only for finite things. How does the infinite nature of Allah’s mercy and your potential for growth change how you view your own shortcomings? What specific comparison will you stop making today?
?
If you believe Allah’s attributes are infinite, why do you sometimes act as if His mercy is limited by your sins? The lesson shows that even a multiple cannot exhaust an infinite series. Will you repent of one sin today, trusting that His mercy is truly infinite and not diminished by your failures?
?
Do you treat your blessings as finite commodities to be hoarded? The one-to-one correspondence between infinite sets suggests that even if others seem to have more, you are not lacking in the sight of Allah. What blessing will you count as truly infinite today, and how will you share it with someone else?

Matter as Substrate and the Rule of Two Forms
Definition of matter as substrate and the rule about two forms in one or two degrees.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that matter is defined as the substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form, and that matter cannot be constituted by two forms in the same degree of composition, but can have forms in different degrees.

Core Idea
Matter is the receptive foundation; form is the shaping principle.

This lesson introduces the foundational definitions of matter and form. The author establishes that matter serves as the passive substrate and receptacle, while form is linked to efficient causation through an agent or part of an agent. A key rule is also presented: matter cannot have two forms in the same compositional degree, whether independent or combined, but it can have forms in different degrees, such as the corporeal form and the specific form.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition passage
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through the example of corporeal and specific forms. Focus on the three roles of matter: substrate, receptacle, bearer.
The phrase 'محل وقابل وحامل' is a standard description of matter.
2
Understand form's relation to agency
Note that form is said to have an agent or part of an agent. This means form participates in efficient causation, but does not act independently.
This sets up the causal explanation in the next lesson.
3
Examine the rule about two forms
The author states that matter cannot be constituted by two forms in one degree. Consider why that would make the composite not one whole. Then see the exception: two forms in different degrees are allowed, giving the example of the corporeal form and the specific form.
Think of the corporeal form as the general shape and the specific form as the unique essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث السابع المادة للصورة محل وقابل وحامل (قال : المادة للصورة محل وقابل وحامل، والصورة لها فاعل أو جزء فاعل ولا تتقوم المادة بصورتين في درجة إما استقلالاً فظاهر وإما اجتماعاً، فلأن القوم حينئذ هو المجموع ، وهو واحد، ويجوز في درجتين كالصورة الجسمية والنوعية . لما كانت الجزئية معتبرة في مفهومي المادة والصورة لم يكونا مادة وصورة إلا باعتبار الإضافة إلى المركب منهما وإما باعتبار إضافة كل منهما إلى الأخرى، فالمادة محل وقابل وحامل للصورة والصورة جزء فاعل لها بمعنى أن فيضان وجود المادة عن الفاعل يكون بإعانة من الصورة ضرورة احتياج المادة إليها مع امتناع استقلالها بالعلية لأن المادة إنما تحتاج إلى الصورة من حيث هي صورة، وإلا من حيث هي تلك الصورة المعينة ضرورة بقائها عند انعدام الصورة المعينة والصورة من حيث هي صورة مالا تكون واحدة بالعدد فلا يمكن أن تكون علة مستقلة للمادة الواحدة بالعدد، وإنما لم يجعلوا المادة جزء فاعل للصورة بناء على احتياج الصورة إليها لما تقرر عندهم من أن شأن المادة القبول لا الفعل .
The seventh investigation: matter as substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form. He said: matter is substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form, and form has an agent or part of an agent. Matter is not constituted by two forms in one degree – either independently, that is apparent, or in combination, for then the composite is the whole, and it is one. And it is permissible in two degrees like the corporeal form and the specific form. Since partiality is considered in the concepts of matter and form, they are not matter and form except by virtue of the relation to the composite of the two or by virtue of the relation of each to the other. So matter is substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form, and form is a part of its agent in the sense that the emanation of the existence of matter from the agent occurs with the assistance of form due to the necessity of matter's need for it, along with the impossibility of its being independent in causality, because matter only needs form insofar as it is form, not insofar as it is that specific form, because of its [matter's] remaining when that specific form ceases to exist. And form insofar as it is form is not one in number, so it cannot be an independent cause for a matter that is one in number. And they did not make matter a part of the agent for form based on form's need for it, because it is established with them that the function of matter is reception, not action.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage defines the relationship between matter and form. It begins by stating that matter is the substrate, receptacle, and bearer for form. Form has an agent or is part of an agent (meaning it contributes to efficient causation). The author explains that matter cannot be constituted by two forms in the same degree, either independently (obviously) or together, because then the composite would be one whole. However, it can have forms in different degrees, like the corporeal form (الصورة الجسمية) and the specific form (الصورة النوعية). He then notes that the concepts of matter and form involve partiality: they are only considered matter and form relative to the composite or to each other. Thus, matter is the substrate for form, and form is a part of the efficient cause of matter: the agent's emanation of matter's existence is assisted by the form, because matter needs form. Matter needs form as form, not as a specific form, because matter remains when the specific form passes away. Form as form is not numerically one, so it cannot be the independent cause of numerically one matter. Finally, matter is not made a part of the efficient cause of form because form also needs matter, but matter's role is reception (القبول), not action (الفعل).

المادة (matter)
الصورة (form)
محل (substrate)
قابل (receptacle)
حامل (bearer)
فاعل (agent)
جزء فاعل (part of an agent)
الصورة الجسمية (corporeal form)
الصورة النوعية (specific form)

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a role of matter?
A) Substrate (محل)
B) Agent (فاعل)
C) Receptacle (قابل)

Key Takeaway
Matter is the receptive substrate; form is associated with agency, and matter cannot have two forms in the same degree of composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that matter cannot hold two incompatible forms at once. What are the competing 'forms' in your own life—ambitions, attachments, distractions—that you try to combine in a single pursuit? Which one must you let go to become a coherent whole?
?
The passage says form has an agent or part of an agent. When you look at your own existence, do you see yourself as mere matter shaped by circumstance, or do you recognize the Divine Agent who gave you your specific form? How will you live today as a conscious bearer of that form?

Form as Partial Efficient Cause of Matter
Explanation of form as partial efficient cause and matter's receptive role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how form acts as a partial efficient cause for matter, why matter needs form as form (not a specific form), why form cannot be an independent cause, and why matter's role is reception, not action.

Core Idea
Form assists in causing matter, but matter's sole function is to receive.

This lesson delves into the causal relationship between matter and form. The author explains that form is a part of the efficient cause of matter: the emanation of matter's existence from the agent is aided by form because matter needs form. However, this need is for form as such, not for any particular specific form, since matter persists even when a specific form is lost. Moreover, form as such is not numerically one, so it cannot independently cause a numerically one matter. Finally, the author clarifies why matter is not made a part of the efficient cause of form: matter's role is to receive, not to act.

Guided Path
1
Read the causal argument
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'لما كانت الجزئية معتبرة'. Focus on the reasoning about form's role in efficient causation.
This part builds on the definitions from the first lesson.
2
Understand form as part of the efficient cause
The author says that the emanation (فيضان) of matter's existence from the agent occurs with the assistance (بإعانة) of form. This means form contributes to the causation of matter.
Form is not the full cause, but a partial efficient cause.
3
Distinguish need for form as such vs. specific form
Matter needs form in general, not a particular form, because matter remains when a specific form (like the form of fire) ceases to exist. If it needed the specific form, it would cease to exist when that form disappears.
This shows matter's ability to receive different forms.
4
Understand why form cannot be an independent cause
Form as form is not numerically one; it is a universal concept. Therefore it cannot be the cause of a numerically one (particular) matter. The cause must be particular.
This is a logical argument based on the one-to-one correspondence of cause and effect.
5
Learn why matter is not an agent for form
Although form needs matter as a substrate, the philosophers do not call matter a part of the agent for form because matter's essential function is reception (القبول), not action (الفعل).
This reinforces the asymmetry between matter and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that matter and form are each independent causes of the composite.

Key Takeaway
Form is a partial efficient cause of matter, but matter's role is strictly receptive, not active.

?

Identify one area of your life where you can consciously acknowledge this dependence before acting.
?
Your heart is like matter—receptive, not active, in receiving guidance or truth. In what specific situation today will you choose to be more like matter (humble and open) rather than trying to be the form (the shaper and controller)? Commit to one concrete response.
