Third Argument Against Time: Non-Existence After Existence
Third argument against time's existence and its refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against time's existence and how it is refuted by distinguishing between temporal afterness and an instantaneous endpoint, and between perpetuity and necessary existence.

Core Idea
Non-existence after existence does not prove necessity; it may be at an instant.

This passage presents the third argument against time's existence from the impossibility of its non-existence after existence, which would imply necessary existence. However, the refutation shows that non-existence after existence can occur at an instant, and even if perpetual, it does not entail necessity. Let's examine the argument and its rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from premise to conclusion to refutation.
Pay attention to the term 'al-ba'diyya' (afterness).
2
Identify the premise about afterness
The argument claims that 'afterness' itself is temporal, so if time's non-existence comes after its existence, that afterness requires another time.
This leads to the problem of time having a time.
3
Understand the conclusion and its absurdity
The argument concludes that time would be necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), which is absurd because time is composite and perishing.
Contrast time's attributes with the attributes of the Necessary Existent.
4
Examine the first part of the answer
The answer rejects that afterness must be temporal; it can be at an instant (al-aan) that is the endpoint of elapsed time.
This breaks the need for another time.
5
Examine the second part of the answer
Even if the afterness is granted, impossibility of non-existence after existence only implies perpetuity (al-dawam), not necessary existence (al-wujub).
Perpetuity is not the same as ontological necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الثالث : الوجه الثالث أنه لو وجد الزمان لامتنع عدمه بعد الوجود لأن هذه البعدية لا تكون إلا زمانية لأن المتأخر لا يجامع المتقدم فيلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان لأن هذا ليس من قبيل التقدم والتأخر فيما بين أجزاء الزمان للقطع بأنه ليس بذاتي وإذا امتنع عدمه كان واجب الوجود وهو محال لأنه متركب يقبل الانقسام ومنقضي يحدث، وتنقض أجزاؤه شيئاً فشيئاً والواجب ليس كذلك . وأجيب بأن كون العدم بعد الوجود لا يقتضي أن يكون في زمان بل يجوز أن يكون في الآن الذي هو طرف للزمان الذي مضى وانقضى أعني الطرف الذي به انقطع الزمان، ولو سلم فامتناع العدم بعد الوجود لا يقتضي الوجوب.
He said: The third: the third aspect (al-wajh al-thalith) is that if time existed, its non-existence after existence would be impossible, because this 'afterness' (al-ba'diyya) can only be temporal, since the later does not coexist with the earlier. So it would entail that time has a time, because this is not of the kind of priority and posteriority among the parts of time, since it is certain that it is not essential. And if its non-existence is impossible, then it would be necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), which is absurd because it is composite, subject to division, and perishing, coming to be, its parts pass away little by little, and the necessary is not like that. And it is answered: That non-existence after existence does not require that it be in time; rather, it is permissible that it be in the instant (al-aan) which is the extremity of the time that has passed and elapsed, I mean the extremity by which time is cut off. And even if granted, the impossibility of non-existence after existence does not entail necessity (al-wujub).
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument (al-wajh al-thalith) against the existence of time and its refutation. The argument: If time existed, its non-existence after existence would be impossible, because 'afterness' is a temporal relation requiring a time for the posterior not to coexist with the prior. This would imply that time has its own time, which is absurd because time's parts are not essentially related that way. Since non-existence after existence is impossible, time would be necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud). But time is composite, divisible, and perishing, unlike the necessary. Hence time cannot exist. The answer refutes: Non-existence after existence does not require a temporal framework; it can be in an instant (al-aan) that is the endpoint of the elapsed time. Moreover, even if the impossibility of non-existence were granted, it would only imply perpetuity (al-dawam), not necessary existence (al-wujub). Thus the argument fails.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That impossibility of non-existence after existence necessarily implies necessary existence (wajib al-wujud).
Correction: Impossibility of non-existence after existence only implies perpetuity (al-dawam), not necessary existence. Moreover, the afterness may be at an instant, not requiring a temporal framework.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The third argument against time fails because non-existence after existence can be at an instant, and perpetuity does not entail necessary existence.

The Third Argument Against Time and Its Refutation
Al-Taftazani presents the third argument against time and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third kalam argument against the existence of time and how it is refuted by distinguishing between temporal posteriority and the instant, and between necessary existence and perpetual renewal.

Core Idea
Time's impossibility of non-existence does not prove it is necessary, only that it may be perpetual.

This lesson presents a sophisticated kalam argument that seeks to prove the impossibility of time by reducing it to a necessary existent, which contradicts its composite nature. Al-Taftazani then provides a subtle refutation that dismantles the argument by clarifying where non-existence occurs and what necessity really entails. Let's examine the argument and its response step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third argument's logical structure
Read the argument carefully. It claims: if time existed, its non-existence after existence would be impossible because such posteriority is necessarily temporal. This leads to an infinite regress (time would have a time) and forces time to be necessary, but time is composite and divisible, so it cannot be necessary. Thus time is impossible.
Notice the key move: they equate impossibility of non-existence with necessary existence.
2
Identify the key concepts: posteriority, instant, essential necessity
Focus on the terms 'البعدية' (posteriority), 'الآن' (the instant), and 'الوجوب الذاتي' (essential necessity). The refutation hinges on distinguishing temporal posteriority from non-temporal posteriority, and necessary existence from perpetual existence by renewal.
The instant (al-an) is not a duration but a boundary.
3
Follow the refutation's two-part answer
First, the refutation says non-existence after existence does not have to be in time; it can be at the instant marking the end of time. Second, even if granted, impossibility of non-existence does not prove essential necessity—it could be perpetual existence through renewal of parts, which is not impossible for time.
The refutation shows the argument fails on two independent grounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : الوجه الثالث : لو وجد الزمان لامتنع عدمه بعد الوجود لأن هذه البعدية لا تكون إلا زمانية لأن المتأخر لا يجامع المتقدم فيلزم أن يكون للزمان زمان لأن هذا ليس من قبيل التقدم والتأخر فيما بين أجزاء الزمان للقطع بأنه ليس بذاتي وإذا امتنع عدمه كان واجب الوجود وهو محال لأنه متركب يقبل الانقسام ومنقضي يحدث، وتنقض أجزاؤه شيئاً فشيئاً والواجب ليس كذلك . وأجيب بأن كون العدم بعد الوجود لا يقتضي أن يكون في زمان بل يجوز أن يكون في الآن الذي هو طرف للزمان الذي مضى وانقضى أعني الطرف الذي به انقطع الزمان، ولو سلم فامتناع العدم بعد الوجود لا يقتضي الوجوب الذاتي المنافي للتركيب والتقضي لجواز لا يقتضي الوجود نظراً إلى ذاته، غايته أن يكون دائماً بتجدد الأجزاء على سبيل الاستمرار، ولا استحالة فيه
He said: The third argument: If time existed, its non-existence after existence would be impossible, because this posteriority (al-bu'diyyah) cannot be except temporal (zamaniyyah), because the posterior does not coexist with the prior. Thus it would follow that time has a time, because this is not of the kind of priority and posteriority among the parts of time, since it is certain that it is not essential. And if its non-existence is impossible, it would be necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud), which is impossible because it is composite (murakkab) admitting division, and divisible (munqadi) that occurs, and its parts pass away little by little, and the Necessary is not like that. And it was answered: That non-existence after existence does not necessitate that it be in time, but it is permissible that it be in the instant (al-an) which is the extremity of the time that has passed and elapsed, meaning the extremity by which time was cut off. And even if it is granted, then the impossibility of non-existence after existence does not necessitate essential necessity (al-wujub al-dhati) which negates composition (al-tarkib) and divisibility (al-taqaddi), because it is possible that it does not necessitate existence in itself; at most it would be perpetual by the renewal of parts in the manner of continuity, and there is no impossibility in that.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a refutation of the third argument against the existence of time. The argument claims: if time existed, its non-existence after existence would be impossible because such posteriority is necessarily temporal, leading to an infinite regress of time having a time. Since impossibility of non-existence entails necessary existence (wajib al-wujud), time would be necessary, but it is composite and divisible, which is impossible for the Necessary. The answer distinguishes: the posteriority of non-existence after existence need not be in time; it can be at an instant that is the endpoint of elapsed time. Even if granted, impossibility of non-existence does not prove essential necessity—only perpetual existence through renewal of parts, which is not impossible.

Key Terms
الزمان
البعدية
الآن
الوجوب الذاتي
التركيب
التقضي
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Impossibility of non-existence after existence always implies essential necessity (wujub dhati).
Correction: It may only imply perpetual existence through renewal of parts, which is not the same as essential necessity.

Checkpoint

A) In a second time
B) At an instant that is the endpoint of time
C) In a timeless realm
D) In the future

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of non-existence after existence does not force time to be necessary; it can be perpetual through renewal of parts, and the posteriority can be at an instant, not necessarily in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does the refutation's idea of perpetual renewal challenge your attachment to static, unchanging states in your spiritual life?

Does that thought make you more aware of your absolute contingency? How can you live today in light of the fact that your existence is not necessary but given by the One who alone is necessary?

Philosophical Proof for Time from Motion and Its Refutation
First philosophical proof for time from motion and response to circularity objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first philosophical proof for time based on motion and the response that the judgment of simultaneity is not dependent on time.

Core Idea
Time inferred from motion does not prove its external reality.

Philosophers argued that comparing two motions in distance implies a quantitative measure beyond speed, distance, and motion—this they call time. al-Taftazani presents this proof and then addresses the objection that the very concepts of simultaneity and speed presuppose time, thereby causing circular reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage describing two motions in a distance and the inference of a third quantitative matter.
Note the conditions: same cessation, speed, and beginning.
2
Understand the inference
See how the difference in traversal leads to a 'quantitative matter' not reducible to speed, distance, or the moving object.
This quantitative matter is claimed to be time.
3
Examine the objection
The objection says that judging simultaneity and delay already assumes time, leading to circularity.
This is a common philosophical critique.
4
See the refutation
al-Taftazani responds that deniers of time still use these concepts without accepting time as a real entity.
This shows the proof does not coerce belief in time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وأثبتته الفلاسفة بوجهين : الأول : أنا إذا فرضنا في مسافة حركتين متوافقتين في الانقطاع فإن توافقتا في السرعة والابتداء أيضاً قطعتا معاً، وإن تأخر ابتداء الثانية أو كانت أبطأ قطعت أقل، فبين طرفي الأول إمكان قطع مسافة معينة بسرعة معينة، وأقل منهما ببطء معين وبين طرفي الثانية إمكان أقل من ذلك بتلك السرعة فهناك أمر مقداري لا يرجع إلى السرعة أو امتداد المسافة أو المتحرك هو المعنى بالزمان، فإن قيل الحكم بالمعية والتأخر والسرعة فرع وجود الزمان فيدور . قلنا : ممنوع فإن المنكرين قاطعون بهذه المعاني . الثاني: تقدم الأب على الابن ضروري وليس وجود الأب وهو ظاهر ولا مع عدم الابن لأنه قد يكون لاحقاً ولا تقدم فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما يلحقه التقدمية والتأخرية بذاته بحيث أنه لا يصير قبله بعد ولا بعد قبل، وهو المراد بالزمان . وأجيب بأن هذه الإمكانات والقبلية اعتبارات عقلية يتصف بها الأعدام، فإن ما بين اليوم وأول السنة أو الشهر متفاوت وعدم الحادث متقدم.
He said: The philosophers proved it by two proofs: The first: If we suppose in a distance two motions agreeing in cessation, then if they also agree in speed and beginning, they both traverse together; and if the beginning of the second is delayed or it is slower, it traverses less. So there is, between the two endpoints of the first, the possibility of traversing a given distance at a given speed, and less than them at a given slowness; and between the two endpoints of the second, the possibility of less than that at that speed. So there is a quantitative matter (amr miqdari) that does not refer to speed, extension of distance, or the moving object; that is the meaning of time (al-zaman). If it is said: The judgment of simultaneity, delay, and speed is a branch of the existence of time, so it would be circular. We say: Denied, for the deniers are certain of these meanings. The second: The priority of the father over the son is necessary. It is not the existence of the father, which is apparent, nor with the non-existence of the son, because he may be later and there is no priority. Thus one must arrive at something to which priority and posteriority attach essentially, such that it does not become before after nor after before; that is the meaning of time. And it was answered: These possibilities and priority are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyyah) that characterize non-existents, for what is between today and the beginning of the year or month is differentiated, and the non-existence of the occurrent is prior.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents two philosophical proofs for the existence of time and the refutation. The first proof: by comparing two motions in a distance, a quantitative measure is inferred that is distinct from speed, distance, and the moving object—this is time. A possible objection of circularity (because judgments of simultaneity presuppose time) is rejected, as deniers still use these concepts. The second proof: the necessary priority of father over son requires an essential priority-posteriority relation, which is time. The answer: all these possibilities and priority are merely mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyyah) applied to non-existents, not external realities; the difference between today and the start of the year is a mental differentiation of non-existence.

المسافة
حركتين
المعية
التأخر
السرعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophical proof from motion conclusively proves time as an external reality.
Correction: The proof only infers a mental concept; the response shows that the reasoning is not coercive.

Checkpoint
What is the main counterargument to the first philosophical proof for time?
A) Time does not exist at all.
B) The inference is based on a false assumption.
C) The judgment of simultaneity does not depend on time.
D) The proof is circular, but the author rejects that objection.

Key Takeaway
The first proof for time from motion is refuted because its key concepts are known even to those who deny time's external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You measure your life by seconds, minutes, and hours—yet this lesson teaches that time is merely a mental construct derived from motion, not an independent reality. If time has no real power, but only exists as an accident of Allah’s creation, will you stop being a slave to the clock and instead see every moment as directly sustained by Him? How would your relationship with your daily schedule change if you truly believed that time is not a resource you own but a veil hiding the constant act of creation?
?
The circularity objection fails because even atheists are certain of simultaneity and delay—concepts that seem to imply time. But you, who believe in the Creator, know that these ideas are simply perceptions of Allah’s creative act. Are you aware, in your daily rush, that your sense of 'running out of time' is an illusion? Will you today, in the face of this truth, repent for treating time as a master and instead use it as a servant to draw closer to Allah?
?
The philosophers built a proof on two motions ending together, but the refutation shows their inference is weak. Yet how many of your own beliefs about your life—your plans, regrets, and ambitions—rest on assumptions about time that you have never questioned? What would it take for you to dismantle those assumptions and live as if each instant is a fresh creation from Allah, uncaused by the past? Is your heart prepared to surrender its attachment to a timeline it does not control?

Philosophical Proof for Time from Priority and Its Refutation
Second philosophical proof for time from priority and its refutation as mental considerations

Learning Objective

The Second Philosophical Proof for Time: Priority and Mental Considerations
Al-Taftazani presents the second proof from priority of father over son, with a refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second philosophical argument for the existence of time based on the priority of father over son, and how it was refuted by the Asharite mutakallimun as mere mental considerations.

Core Idea
Priority and posteriority are mental categories, not real entities.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents the second proof that philosophers use to argue for time as a real entity. They argue that the necessary priority of a father over his son cannot be explained by existence or non-existence alone, so time must be real. However, the answer shows that such relations are merely mental considerations, not objective realities. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow of the proof and its refutation.
The passage is brief but dense. Take your time.
2
Identify the proof's structure
The proof has three premises: priority is necessary, it is not the father's existence, nor the son's non-existence. Then it concludes there must be an entity (time) that grounds priority.
The phrase 'لا بد من الانتهاء' signals the conclusion.
3
Understand the refutation
The refutation argues that priority is a mental consideration (i'tibar 'aqli) that applies to non-existents. The example of the interval between today and the beginning of the year illustrates that such relations are not real.
The term 'اعتبارات عقلية' is key.
4
Reflect on the broader implication
Consider why this refutation matters in kalam: it denies that time is a real entity independent of created events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: تقدم الأب على الابن ضروري وليس وجود الأب وهو ظاهر ولا مع عدم الابن لأنه قد يكون لاحقاً ولا تقدم فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى ما يلحقه التقدمية والتأخرية بذاته بحيث أنه لا يصير قبله بعد ولا بعد قبل، وهو المراد بالزمان . وأجيب بأن هذه الإمكانات والقبلية اعتبارات عقلية يتصف بها الأعدام، فإن ما بين اليوم وأول السنة أو الشهر متفاوت وعدم الحادث متقدم .
Second: The priority of the father over the son is necessary, and the existence of the father [is not priority] — it is apparent — nor does it [exist] with the non-existence of the son, because the father may be later and there is no priority. So it is necessary to end with something to which priority and posteriority attach in themselves, such that it does not become before after nor after before, and that is what is meant by time. And it was answered: That these possibilities and priority are mental considerations that characterize non-existences, for what is between today and the beginning of the year or the month is different, and the non-existence of the contingent is prior.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophical proof for time, based on the necessary priority of a father over his son. The priority is not simply the existence of the father, nor the non-existence of the son, because the father might be later in existence. So we must posit something that grounds priority and posteriority in itself, which is time. The answer given is that such relations (priority, possibility) are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya) that apply to non-existents. For example, the interval between today and the beginning of the year is different, and the non-existence of a contingent event is prior. So the proof fails because these are mental, not real.

الأعدام

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the priority of father over son is a real, objective relation independent of the mind.
Correction: The refutation shows that priority and similar relations are mental considerations (i'tibarat 'aqliyya) that apply to non-existents, not real entities.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If priority is only a mental consideration, then your feeling that 'I should have done something earlier' is a mental construct, not an objective reality. How does this affect your regret over past sins? Will you let that regret hold you back from turning to Allah now, knowing that the 'earlier' you think you lost is not real? Commit to acting immediately on one good deed you've been postponing.
?
The passage says that the non-existence of a contingent event is prior. This means that before you existed, you were a non-existence that had priority over your existence. Does that humble you? How does realizing you were nothing for an eternity, and that your existence is a temporary gift, change your attachment to worldly status, health, or relationships? What are you clinging to as if it will always be there?
?
You often think of time as a container for your actions—'I don't have enough time,' 'I'll do it later.' But this lesson suggests time itself is a mental construct. What if the real issue is not lacking time, but lacking presence with Allah? Are you using 'time' as an excuse to delay repentance, learning, or prayer? Decide today: when you catch yourself thinking 'later,' replace it with 'now, with Allah's help.'

Objection against the philosophers' proof of time
Objection that the proof is based on unsound principles, with partial response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of a dialectical objection against the philosophical proof for time, including the list of allegedly corrupt principles and the partial rebuttal.

Core Idea
Objections can attack the foundations of a proof without rejecting its conclusion.

This passage presents a theological objection to the philosophers' argument for the definition of time. Instead of directly challenging the conclusion, the objection targets the underlying principles—such as the indivisible part and the nature of motion—on which the proof rests. The response is partial and dialectical, showing how debates in kalam often proceed by questioning premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic source and its translation carefully. Notice the dense list of 'corrupt principles' that the objector claims the proof depends on.
The passage is a single continuous argument; try to follow its logical flow.
2
Identify the corrupt principles
List the four principles mentioned as unsound: the indivisible part, connecting motions, annihilation of time, and the necessity of a new time upon cessation.
These principles are controversial within Islamic philosophy and kalam.
3
Understand the partial response
The objector then offers a partial rebuttal: even if the principles are accepted, the conclusion does not follow. Focus on the phrase 'we do not accept that...' which challenges the necessity of a subject.
The response is incomplete; the text ends abruptly with a rejection of the claim of necessity.
4
Reflect on the dialectical context
Consider why the objector lists these principles. This is a typical kalam strategy: to show that a proof rests on unproven or disputed premises.
The objection does not fully refute the proof; it merely exposes its dependence on specific assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على هذا الدليل بأنه مبني على أصول فاسدة، مثل بطلان الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ومثل امتناع إيصال الحركات ولوزم السكون بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين، ومثل امتناع فناء الزمان، ولزوم أن يكون عدمه بعد الوجود مقتضياً لزمان آخر وبعد ثبوت هذه الأصول بالدليل أو التزام الخصم إياها بأن يجعل هذا احتجاجاً على باقي الفلاسفة فلا نسلم أن القابل للتفاوت، أو يلزم أن يكون كما مقتضياً لموضوع ، وإنما يلزم لو كان ذلك (ه) بحب الذات وهو ممنوع ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة
And (someone) objected to this proof that it is built on corrupt principles, such as the invalidity of the indivisible part, and the impossibility of connecting motions and the necessity of rest between every two rectilinear motions, and the impossibility of the annihilation of time, and the necessity that its non-existence after existence would entail another time. And after these principles are established by proof or the opponent's commitment to them, (then) this is made an argument against the rest of the philosophers: we do not accept that the thing receptive to difference (al-qābil li al-tafāwut) necessarily requires a subject, and it would only be necessary if that were by the essence (bi-ḥubb al-dhāt?) which is denied, and the claim of necessity is not heard.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents an objection (i‘tirāḍ) against the philosophers' proof for the definition of time. The objection lists several allegedly corrupt principles (uṣūl fāsida) on which the proof relies: (1) the invalidity of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā'), (2) the impossibility of connecting motions (īṣāl al-ḥarakāt) with the necessity of rest (sukūn) between every two rectilinear motions, (3) the impossibility of the annihilation (fanā') of time, and (4) the necessity that if time ceased after existence, it would require another time. Then the objection seems to argue that even if the opponent commits to these principles, they do not necessarily lead to the conclusion. The phrase 'we do not accept that the receptive to difference necessarily requires a subject' (fala nusallim an al-qābil li al-tafāwut aw yalzam an yakūn kamā muqtaḍan li mawḍū‘) is partially obscure. It may mean that the property of being measurable (al-qābil li al-tafāwut) does not necessarily entail a subject (mawḍū‘) like motion. The final claim is that the assertion of necessity (da‘wā al-ḍarūra) is not accepted (ghayr masmū‘a). This objection thus challenges the philosophers' reasoning from quantity and continuity to a specific subject.

Key Terms
الاعتراض (al-i‘tirāḍ)
الأصول الفاسدة (al-uṣūl al-fāsida)
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazzā')
فناء الزمان (fanā' al-zamān)
السكون (al-sukūn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection completely refutes the philosophers' proof of time.
Correction: The text only presents the objection and a partial response; it does not fully refute the proof. The objection aims to show the proof's reliance on disputed premises, leaving the debate open.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a corrupt principle in the objection?
A) The invalidity of the indivisible part
B) The impossibility of connecting rectilinear motions
C) The eternity of time
D) The impossibility of the annihilation of time

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the philosophers' proof by attacking its foundational principles, but the text only gives a partial response, highlighting the dialectical nature of kalam debates.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a claim as self-evident without examining its foundations? In which area of your beliefs might you be guilty of that?
?
The objector challenges the 'self-evidence' of the philosophers. In your own life, what beliefs do you hold as self-evident that might actually rest on shaky principles?
?
The passage shows that objections can attack foundations without rejecting the conclusion. How might this apply to your own intellectual humility—are you willing to consider objections that don't directly refute your positions but question their basis?

Responding to the Objection of False Principles
Al-Taftazani responds to an objection about false principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends his proof against the charge that it rests on false philosophical principles.

Core Idea
An objection based on false principles fails even if the principles are accepted.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses a common objection: that his argument for the existence of God relies on principles rejected by some philosophers. He shows that even if those principles were true, the objection does not hold. This lesson unpacks his logical response.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: the objector lists several false principles, then the author gives a conditional response.
The objection is introduced by 'واعترض'.
2
Identify the false principles
The objector claims the proof is based on false principles. Find and list them: indivisible part, connection of motions, rest between motions, annihilation of time, etc.
3
Understand the author's conditional argument
The author says: even if these principles are established by proof or accepted by the opponent, the objection does not follow. He denies that the measure is subject to variation or requires a subject.
The key phrase: 'فلا نسلم أن القابل للتفاوت'.
4
Grasp the final point
The author concludes that the necessity claimed by the objector is not conceded. The claim of necessity is unsupported.
The objection is rebutted without conceding the principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض على هذا الدليل بأنه مبني على أصول فاسدة، مثل بطلان الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ومثل امتناع اتصال الحركات ولوازم السكون بين كل حركتين مستقيمتين، ومثل امتناع فناء الزمان، ولزوم أن يكون عدمه بعد الوجود مقتضياً لزمان آخر وبعد ثبوت هذه الأصول بالدليل أو التزام الخصم إياها بأن يجعل هذا احتجاجاً على باقي الفلاسفة فلا نسلم أن القابل للتفاوت، أو يلزم أن يكون كما مقتضياً لموضوع ، وإنما يلزم لو كان ذلك بحب الذات وهو ممنوع ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة.
And it was objected against this proof that it is based on false principles, such as the invalidity of the indivisible part, and the impossibility of the connection of motions and the necessities of rest between every two straight motions, and the impossibility of the annihilation of time, and the necessity that its non-existence after existence would require another time. And after establishing these principles by proof or the opponent's acceptance of them (so that he makes this a refutation against the rest of the philosophers), we do not accept that the measure is subject to variation, or that it necessarily requires a subject. It would only necessitate if that were by essence, which is denied. And the claim of necessity is not heard.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and the author's response. The objector says the proof relies on false principles like the denial of atoms, impossibility of continuous motion, impossibility of the annihilation of time, etc. The author responds: Even if these principles are taken as true (either proven or conceded by the opponent to refute other philosophers), the conclusion does not follow. Specifically, the objector's claim that the measure is subject to variation or requires a subject is not conceded. The necessity would only follow if it were essential, which is denied. And the objector's claim of necessity is unsupported.

Key Terms
أصول فاسدة
جزء لا يتجزأ
اتصال الحركات
سكون
فناء الزمان
قابل للتفاوت
مقتض لموضوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a proof uses principles that the opponent rejects, the proof is automatically invalid.
Correction: Even if the principles are false or rejected, the proof may still be valid if the objection's conclusion does not follow from those principles. The author shows that even assuming the principles, the objection fails.

Checkpoint
What is the author's response to the objection that his proof is based on false principles?
A) He concedes that the principles are false.
B) He argues that even if the principles are true, the objection does not follow.
C) He says the objection is irrelevant.
D) He proves the principles are true.

Key Takeaway
The objection fails even if its principles are accepted because the conclusion does not necessarily follow.

Can you see the flaw in their assumptions even if you concede them for argument? Examine one area of your faith where you might be accepting worldly 'false principles' without realizing it. Will you commit to identifying and rejecting one such assumption this week?
?
The author points out that the objector's claim of self-evidence is not accepted. Are there beliefs in your own life—about success, happiness, or your own worth—that you treat as self-evident but are actually built on false principles? How would your life change if you honestly tested those assumptions against the truth of the Quran and Sunnah? Write down one assumption you are ready to challenge today.

First Objection: Unchanging Entities and Time
First objection and answer on non-changing entities in time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection to the definition of time as the measure of motion, and the answer that distinguishes between co-occurrence and inherence, introducing the concepts of dahr and sarmad.

Core Idea
Unchanging beings relate to time by co-occurrence, not inherence.

One of the strongest objections to the philosophical definition of time is that unchanging entities (like God, souls, or even a resting body) are described as being 'yesterday' or 'today,' just like moving things. How can time be the measure of only change if it applies to the unchanging? Taftazani presents the objection and then gives a precise answer that redefines how we relate the unchanging to time.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice that the objection claims non-changing things are described with temporal terms like 'yesterday' and 'today', so time cannot be only for change.
Think about why this would be a problem for the definition.
2
Read the answer
The answer says the non-changing is related to time by 'co-occurrence' (husul ma'ahu) not 'inherence' (fihi). Understand the distinction.
Co-occurrence means they exist together without one being inside the other.
3
Understand the three relations
The answer introduces a tripartite scheme: relation of changing to changing = time; of constant to changing = dahr; of constant to constant = sarmad.
This clarifies that time is specific to change.
4
Reflect on the significance
This shows that time is not the measure of all existence but specifically of change, while other terms cover other relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقض أدلة الفلاسفة
قال : ثم عروض بوجوه :
الأول: أن غير المتغير كالجسم وسكونه بل الواجب وجميع المجردات يتصف بالكون في الأمس واليوم والغد والحركة من غير فرق، وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس مقداراً للوجود الأن المتغير لا ينطبق على الثابت وبالعكس .
الثاني : أن الحركة بمعنى الكون في الوسط ثابت فمقداره لا يكون متغيراً و بمعنى الممتد من المبدأ إلى المنتهي وهمي فمقداره لا يكون موجداً .
الثالث: أن ثبوت العرض مع عدم محله بديهي الاستحالة بخلاف ما نسميه الزمان مع عدم حركة الفلك وبهذا يظهر أنه ليس نفس الفلك ولا حركته .
وأجيب عن الأول بأن غير المتغير إنما ينسب (۱) إذا نسب إلى الزمان بالحصول معه لا فيه فنسبة المتغير إلى المغير هو الزمان ونسبة الثابت إلى المتغير هو الدهر وإلى الثابت هو السرمد. وعن الثاني بأنه كما لا يجب بل يمتنع في وجود غير القار اجتماع جزءين منه فكذا في وجود مقداره، وعن الثالث بأن مبناه على حكم الوهم والكل ضعيف).
Refutation of the proofs of the philosophers. He said: Then it is objected (to the proof) with objections: First: That the non-changing, like the body and its rest, nay the Necessary Being and all immaterial entities, are described as being in yesterday, today, and tomorrow and in movement without difference, and by this it becomes clear that it (time) is not a measure for existence, because the changing does not correspond to the constant and vice versa. Second: That movement in the sense of being in the middle is constant, so its measure would not be changing, and in the sense of the extended from the beginning to the end is imaginary, so its measure would not be existent. Third: That the subsistence of an accident without its subject is self-evidently impossible, contrary to what we call time without the movement of the sphere, and by this it becomes clear that it is neither the sphere nor its movement. And it was answered concerning the first: That the non-changing is only attributed (1) when it is attributed to time as co-occurring with it, not in it. So the relation of the changing to that which changes it is time, and the relation of the constant to the changing is dahr (eternity), and to the constant is sarmad (perpetuity). Concerning the second: That just as it is not necessary, rather impossible, for the existence of a non-fixed thing to have two parts of it combined, so also in the existence of its measure. Concerning the third: That its foundation is upon the judgment of estimation, and all of it is weak.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents a set of three objections to the definition of time as the measure of motion, followed by their refutations. It is a typical kalam argumentation structure where the author (Taftazani) first states the objections and then provides answers. The first objection argues that unchanging entities (like the Necessary Being, souls, bodies at rest) are described as being in time (yesterday, today) just like changing entities, so time cannot be the measure of only change. The second objection distinguishes two meanings of motion: motion as being in a middle state (constant) and motion as extended from start to end (imaginary). If time is the measure of the first, it would be constant; if of the second, it would be non-existent. The third objection asserts that the subsistence of an accident without its subject is impossible, but we conceive of time existing without the movement of the celestial sphere, so time is neither the sphere nor its motion. Then the answers are given: For the first, the non-changing is related to time as co-occurrence (bi-husul ma'ahu) not inherence (fihi). The relation of changing to changing is time; of constant to changing is dahr; of constant to constant is sarmad. For the second, just as it is impossible for two parts of a non-fixed entity to coexist, so too for its measure. For the third, the objection relies on estimation (wahm) and is weak. The author concludes that all these objections are weak.

Key Terms
زمان
دهر
سرمد
غير المتغير
حصول معه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That being described as 'yesterday' implies inherence in time, making time universal.
Correction: The answer shows that the unchanging 'co-occurs' with time, not inheres, so time remains the measure of change only.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, how is the unchanging entity related to time?
A) It inheres in time.
B) It co-occurs with time.
C) It is outside time entirely.
D) It is measured by time.

Key Takeaway
The unchanging relates to time by co-occurrence, not inherence, preserving the definition of time as the measure of change.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You live in time—every moment you change, grow, or decay. But the One you worship does not change at all. Does your daily practice reflect a living relationship with the Unchanging, or do you treat Him as if He is affected by your shifting moods and circumstances? Commit today to remind yourself: 'He is constant; I am not. I need His steadiness.'
?
This lesson shows that unchanging entities are not inside time but co-occur with it. Your Creator stands outside your timeline, yet He is present with every moment of your life. Does this make you feel distant or intimately close? Shed any thought that God is remote—actively sense His presence this hour.
?
The terms dahr and sarmad describe relationships with the constant. If your soul is meant to find rest in the Eternal, are you seeking permanence in changing things? Identify one attachment in your life that you treat as unchanging but is actually temporal. Break its hold today by affirming that only God remains.

Second Objection: The Dual Meaning of Motion
Second objection and answer on the dual meaning of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection that motion has two meanings (constant and imaginary), and the answer based on the impossibility of coexistence of parts for non-fixed entities.

Core Idea
Motion as constant has a constant measure, motion as imaginary has no existence.

The second objection attacks the definition of time by analyzing the concept of motion itself. If motion means 'being in the middle' it is constant, so its measure would be constant, not temporal. If motion means 'the extended from start to end' it is imaginary, so its measure would be non-existent. Taftazani's answer employs a key kalam principle about non-fixed entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Notice the two senses of motion: as a steady state (being in the middle) and as an imaginary extension (from start to end).
Think why each sense leads to a problem for time as measure.
2
Read the answer
The answer uses the principle that for a non-fixed entity (like motion), it is impossible for two parts to coexist. The same applies to its measure, time.
This refutes the objection by showing the analogy holds.
3
Understand the kalam principle
Non-fixed entities are those that cannot exist all at once, like motion. Their parts occur sequentially. Time as measure shares this property.
This defends time as the measure of motion's flow.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The objection fails because it misconceives the nature of motion and measure.

Third Objection: Time Without Sphere Motion
Third objection and brief answer from estimation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that time seems to exist even without celestial motion, and the answer that such a notion is based on estimation (wahm) and is weak.

Core Idea
Time without motion is a product of estimation, not reality.

The third objection appeals to common sense: we can conceive of time passing even if the celestial sphere were still. Yet if time requires motion, how can we imagine it separately? Taftazani dismisses this objection as relying on the faculty of estimation (wahm), which can produce false judgments. This highlights the need for rigorous rational analysis over mere imagination.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
The objection says it is self-evident that an accident cannot exist without a subject, yet we conceive time without the sphere's motion.
This seems to show time is independent of motion.
2
Read the answer
Taftazani says the objection is based on the judgment of estimation (wahm), which is a weak mental faculty.
Estimation can produce false ideas.
3
Evaluate the strength
The author concludes that all these objections are weak, so the original definition of time stands.
This shows confidence in the philosophical position.
4
Reflect on the role of wahm
Recognize that kalam often distinguishes between rational proof and imaginative estimates.

First Objection: Fixed Entities and Time
Taftazani presents the first objection against time as measure of motion, focusing on fixed entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why defining time as the measure of motion leads to a contradiction regarding fixed entities like the heavens and the Necessary Being.

Core Idea
If time measures change, it cannot also measure fixed things.

The first objection targets a key implication of the definition of time as motion's measure. It argues that if time is essentially changing, it cannot correspond to fixed entities, yet we are certain such entities exist in time. This reveals a fundamental dilemma about the nature of time itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the First Objection
Read the Arabic passage for the first objection and its translation. Note the structure: an implication (ملازمة) and a false consequent (بطلان اللازم).
The objection also refutes Abu al-Barakat's position.
2
Analyze the Implication
Understand why time, as measure of motion, must be changing, and why a changing measure cannot correspond to fixed things.
Key term: 'انطباق' (correspondence).
3
Examine the Consequent
We are certain fixed entities exist yesterday, today, and tomorrow. Denying this would also deny motion's temporality.
4
Reflect on the Dilemma
The measure faces a dilemma: if changing, it cannot match fixed; if fixed, it cannot match changing. This shows the definition's inadequacy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ثم ،عورض أي الدليل المذكور بوجوه :
أحدها: أن الزمان لو كان مقدار الحركة لامتنع انتساب الأمور الثابتة إليه، أما الملازمة فلأنه حينئذ يكون متغيراً غير قار لأن مقدار المتغير أولى بأن يكون متغيراً، والمتغير لا ينطبق على الثابت لأن معنى الانطباق أن يكون جزءاً من هذا مطابقاً لجزء من ذلك على الترتيب في التقدم والتأخر وأما بطلان اللازم فلأنا كما نقطع بأن الحركة موجودة أمس واليوم وغداً، كذلك نقطع بأن السكون بل السماء وغيرها من الموجودات الثابتة حتى الواجب، وجميع المجردات موجودة أمس واليوم وغداً ، وإن جاز إنكار هذا جاز إنكار ذاك، وبهذا الوجه أبطلوا قول أبي البركات إن الباقي لا يتصور بقاؤه إلا في زمان مستمر، وما لا يكون في الزمان ويكون باقياً لا بد أن يكون لبقائه مقدار من الزمان فالزمان مقدار الوجود (۱) ، وذلك لأن المقدار في نفسه إن كان متغيراً استحال انطباقه على الثابت وإن كان ثابتاً استحال انطباقه على (٢) المتغير، وثانيها : أن الحركة كما سيجيء تطلق على كون المتحرك متوسطاً بين المبدأ والمنتهي وهو أمر ثابت مستمر الوجود وعلى الأمر الممتد في المسافة من المبدأ إلى المنتهي وهو وهم محض لا تحقق له في الخارج لعدم تقرر أجزائه، والحركة التي جعل الزمان مقداراً لها إن أخذت بالمعنى الأول لزم كون الزمان قاراً غير سيال وهو محال، وإن أخذت بالمعنى الثاني لم يكن الزمان موجوداً ضرورة امتناع قيام الموجود بالمعدوم، وثالثها: لو كان الزمــان مقدار حركة الفلك لكان تصور وجودها بدونها (۳) تصور محال، واللازم باطل لأنا قاطعون بوجود أمر سيال به القبلية والبعدية والمضي والاستقبال وإن لم يوجد حركة ولا فلك حتى لو تصورنا مدة كان الفلك معدومـاً فيها فوجد، أو ساكناً (٤) فتحرك، أو يعدم فيها الفلك أو حركته ، لم يكن ذلك بمنزلة تصورنا عدم حركة الفلك حال وجودها، وإن أمكن إنكار هذا الأمر(ه) بدون الحركة أمكن إنكاره معها من غير فرق وبالجملة فارتفاع الزمان بارتفاع حركة الفلك ليس بديهياً كارتفاع مقدار الشيء بارتفاعه، ولهذا لم يذهب أحد العقلاء من
He said: Then it is objected, i.e., the aforementioned proof, with objections: One of them: That if time were the measure of motion, then the attribution of fixed things to it would be impossible. As for the implication, this is because then it would be changing and non-fixed, because the measure of a changing thing is more deserving of being changing, and the changing does not correspond to the fixed because the meaning of correspondence is that a part of this corresponds to a part of that in order of priority and posteriority. As for the falsehood of the consequent, because just as we are certain that motion exists yesterday, today, and tomorrow, so too we are certain that rest, nay the heavens and other fixed existents, even the Necessary Being, and all immaterial entities exist yesterday, today, and tomorrow. And if denial of this is permitted, then denial of that is permitted. And by this aspect they refuted the statement of Abi al-Barakat that the permanent cannot be conceived as enduring except in continuous time, and what is not in time but is permanent must have a measure of time for its duration, so time is the measure of existence (1). This is because the measure itself, if it is changing, its correspondence to the fixed is impossible, and if it is fixed, its correspondence to the changing is impossible. Second: That motion, as will come, is applied (i) to the state of the moving being between the beginning and the end, which is a fixed continuous existent, and (ii) to the extended thing in the distance from beginning to end, which is pure imagination with no realization in the external due to the lack of determination of its parts. And the motion of which time is made the measure, if taken in the first sense, then time would be fixed and non-flowing, which is impossible; and if taken in the second sense, then time would not exist, due to the impossibility of an existent subsisting in a non-existent. Third: If time were the measure of the motion of the sphere, then conceiving its existence without it would be conceiving an impossibility. But the consequent is false, because we are certain of the existence of a flowing thing with priority and posteriority, past and future, even if motion and sphere do not exist, so that if we conceive a period in which the sphere was non-existent and then came to be, or was stationary and then moved, or the sphere or its motion ceased in it, that would not be like our conceiving the non-existence of the motion of the sphere while it exists. And if it is possible to deny this thing without motion, then it is possible to deny it with it without difference. In brief, the removal of time with the removal of the sphere's motion is not self-evident like the removal of the measure of a thing with its removal. And for this reason, none of the intellects from ... (incomplete).
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage presents three new objections against the definition of time as the measure of motion. They are introduced as counter-objections (عورض) to the earlier argument. The first objection argues that if time is the measure of motion, then fixed entities (like rest, heavens, Necessary Being) cannot be attributed to it, but we know they are. The implication is that the measure of a changing thing must be changing, and a changing measure cannot correspond to fixed entities because correspondence requires ordered parts. The consequent is false because we are equally certain that fixed entities exist in time. This refutes Abu al-Barakat's claim that permanent things require time for their duration. The objection shows the dilemma: if the measure is changing, it cannot correspond to fixed; if fixed, it cannot correspond to changing. The second objection distinguishes two meanings of motion: the continuous state (fixed) and the extended imaginary thing. If time measures the first, time would be fixed (impossible); if the second, time would be non-existent (impossible). The third objection argues that time is conceivable even without the sphere's motion, so it cannot be identical to its measure. The denial of time without motion is not self-evident. The passage ends mid-sentence, likely continuing with the conclusion.

Key Terms
زمان
مقدار الحركة
الأمور الثابتة
انطباق
أبو البركات
متغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fixed entities like the Necessary Being are not in time.
Correction: The objection asserts that we are certain fixed entities exist yesterday, today, and tomorrow, so they are temporal.

Checkpoint
What dilemma does the first objection present regarding time as a measure?
A) Time must be either measure of motion or measure of rest.
B) Time must be either changing or fixed.
C) Time must be either imaginary or real.
D) Time must be either finite or infinite.

Key Takeaway
The first objection shows that time as measure of motion cannot account for the temporality of fixed entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If even the Necessary Being is described as existing 'yesterday, today, and tomorrow', does your awareness of His constant presence change how you approach Him right now? Will you speak to Him as though He is as present as the next breath?
?
The passage argues that fixed things still require time. Is there an area of your life where you believe you are 'fixed' or unchanging, but in reality, your spiritual state is measured by the passing of days? What will you do this week to break the stagnation?
?
The objection shows that time cannot be reduced to motion alone. In what ways do you reduce your relationship with Allah to mere 'actions' without recognizing the fixed state of your heart? Are you willing to examine your 'stillness' before Him today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Second Objection: Two Meanings of Motion
Taftazani's second objection distinguishes two meanings of motion to refute the time definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ambiguity of the term 'motion' undermines the definition of time as its measure.

Core Idea
Motion has two meanings; one makes time fixed, the other makes it non-existent.

The second objection targets the term 'motion' itself. Taftazani distinguishes two senses: as a continuous state and as an imaginary extension. The objection argues that time, if defined as the measure of motion, must collapse into one of these senses, each leading to an absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Two Meanings
Read the passage and note the two definitions of motion: as a fixed continuous state (أمر ثابت مستمر) and as an imaginary flow (وهم محض).
The second meaning is not externally real.
2
Apply Each to Time
If time measures motion in the first sense, time becomes fixed (قار) not flowing (سيال), which contradicts time's flowing nature. If in the second sense, time would be non-existent because it would subsist in an imaginary thing.
3
Conclude the Objection
Both options are impossible, so time cannot be the measure of motion.
This is a dilemma that refutes the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ثم ،عورض أي الدليل المذكور بوجوه :

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion has only one meaning: physical movement.
Correction: The passage distinguishes a second meaning of motion as an imaginary extension, which is not externally real.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, if 'motion' is taken in the first sense (the continuous state), what would be the consequence for time?
A) Time would be non-existent.
B) Time would be fixed.
C) Time would be flowing.
D) Time would be imaginary.

Key Takeaway
The second objection shows that the ambiguity of 'motion' leads to absurdities for the definition of time as its measure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life's journey from birth to the present, do you experience it as a fixed, continuous state or as a fleeting, imaginary extension? How does this passage's distinction challenge the way you think about the reality of time?
?
If time were truly fixed (as one meaning suggests), would your sense of urgency toward worship and good deeds change? What does your answer reveal about your reliance on time's flow for motivation?
?
This objection shows that our intuitive understanding of motion is ambiguous. In what areas of your faith might you be relying on ambiguous concepts without realizing it? What can you do to anchor your beliefs more firmly in clear, reasoned truths?
